Genetic Toxicology
Joanna Klapacz and B. Bhaskar Gollapudi
GENETIC TOXICOLOGY DEFINITION
Genetic toxicology is the study of genetic damage to the hereditary 
material that results in genetic alterations. Included in this broad 
definition are chemical and physical agents that cause mutagenic-
ity, or transmissible genetic alterations, and genotoxicity. In con-
trast to mutagenicity, genotoxicity covers a broader spectrum of 
endpoints, including DNA damage such as DNA strand breaks and 
DNA adduct biomarkers (both pro-mutagenic and non-mutagenic), 
unscheduled DNA synthesis (UDS), and sister chromatid exchanges 
(SCEs); these are all measures of genotoxicity, as opposed to muta-
genicity because they are not themselves transmissible from cell 
to cell or generation to generation. Genotoxicity also encompasses 
the mechanisms by which DNA damage occurs and the cellular 
responses to that damage. All of these effects can be assessed 
directly by measuring the interaction of agents with DNA or more 
indirectly through the assessment of DNA repair or the produc-
tion of gene mutations or chromosome alterations. Standardized 
assay outcomes that detect mutations in populations of cells and/or 
organisms have been applied to systematic hazard classifications of 
chemicals under the United Nations Globally Harmonized System 
of Classification and Labelling of Chemicals (GHS). Under GHS, 
the chemical hazards associated with germ cell mutagenicity are 
assigned to one of two hazard categories: (1) substances known 
to induce heritable mutations (subcategory 1A) or regarded as if 
they induce heritable mutations (subcategory 1B) in the germ cells 
of humans; and (2) substances which cause concern that they may 
induce heritable mutations in the germ cells of humans (UN, 2015).
It is worth mentioning that in the last decades, there has been 
an increased emphasis on the role of epigenetic changes that con-
trol gene expression through DNA methylation and histone modi-
fications in the production of altered phenotypes (Watson and 
Goodman, 2002; LeBaron et al., 2010; Dearfield et al., 2017). 
Although these epigenetic changes can be transmitted between gen-
erations, these are also fluid and adaptive, are not mutations by defi-
nition as they do not involve changes in DNA sequence, and have

In this chapter, we will discuss the history of the development 
of the field of genetic toxicology, the cellular pathways that counter-
act DNA damage on a daily basis, the use of genetic toxicology data 
in cancer and genetic risk assessments, the mechanisms underlying 
genetic toxicology assays, the assays that can be used for detecting 
genotoxic endpoints, the use of the same assays for better under-
standing mechanisms of mutagenesis, and new methods for the 
assessment of genetic alterations. This field of toxicology is evolving 
rapidly, and a review of its past and present state will set the stage to 
allow for a consideration of what are likely next major landmarks.
HISTORY OF GENETIC TOXICOLOGY
The field of genetic toxicology can be considered to have its roots in 
the pioneering work of H.J. Muller (1927), who showed that x-rays 
could induce mutations in the fruit fly, Drosophila. In his studies, 
he showed not only that radiation exposure could increase the over-
all frequencies of mutations but also that the types of mutations 
induced were exactly the same in effect, or phenotype, as those 
observed in the absence of radiation exposure. Thus, the induced 
mutagenic responses should be assessed in relation to background 
mutations. As a conclusion to this study of radiation-induced muta-
tions, Muller predicted the utility of mutagenesis studies not only 
for the study of mutations themselves but also for gene mapping 
approaches.
Karl Sax (1938) built upon Muller’s original studies of radi-
ation-induced mutations by showing that x-rays could also induce 
structural alterations to chromosomes in Tradescantia pollen grains. 
Sax and his colleagues, notably in the absence of knowledge of 
DNA structure and chromosomal organization, showed that at 
least two critical lesions in a nuclear target are required for the 
production of an exchange within (intrachromosome) or between 
(interchromosome) chromosomes. We know now that the lesions 
identified by Sax are DNA double-strand breaks, base damages, or 
multiply damaged sites that are clusters of damages within one or 
two helical turns of DNA (reviewed by Ward, 1988). In addition, 
Sax and colleagues (Sax, 1939; Sax and Luippold, 1952) showed 
that the yield of chromosome aberrations was reduced if the total 
dose of x-rays was delivered over extended periods of time or split 
into two fractions separated by several hours. These observations 
led to the concept of restitution of radiation-induced damage, which 
was later recognized as involving specific DNA repair processes 
(see below).
Consideration of the genetic effects of exogenous agents on 
cells was expanded to include chemicals in 1946, when Charlotte 
Auerbach and colleagues reported that mustard gas could induce 
mutations in Drosophila and that these mutations were phenotypi-
cally similar to those induced by x-rays (Auerbach and Robson, 
1946). Thus, the field of chemical mutagenesis was initiated to run 
in parallel with studies of radiation mutagenesis. These original 
studies of Auerbach (actually conducted in 1941) are placed in a 
historical and biological perspective by the delightful review of 
Geoffrey Beale (1993).
Although the scientific value of the analysis of mutations in 
Drosophila was clear, there was an impression that the extrapola-
tion to predict similar effects in human populations was too wide 
a step. Thus, a research effort of great magnitude was initiated to 
attempt to assess radiation-induced mutations in mice. This effort 
resulted in the publication by William Russell (1951) of data on 
x-ray–induced mutations using a mouse-specific-locus mutation 
assay. These data clearly showed that the type of results obtained 
with Drosophila could be replicated in a mammalian system. The 
mouse tester strain developed for the specific-locus assay has reces-
sive mutations at seven loci coding for visible mutations, such as 
coat color, eye color, and ear shape. This homozygous recessive 
tester strain can be used for identifying recessive mutations induced 
in wild type genes at the same loci in mice treated with radiation 
or chemical mutagens. It was noteworthy that the mutation rate for 
x-ray–induced mutations in germ cells was similar in mouse and 
Drosophila. Subsequent studies by Liane Russell and colleagues 
showed that chemicals could induce mutations at the same seven 
loci (Russell et al., 1981).
Over the next 20 years, genetic toxicologists investigated the 
induction of mutations and chromosomal alterations in somatic and 
germ cells, largely following exposures to radiation, but increas-
ingly using chemical mutagens as well. The ability to grow cells 
in vitro, either as primary cultures or as transformed cell lines, 
enhanced these quantitative studies. The in vitro culture of human 
lymphocytes, stimulated to re-enter the cell cycle by phytohemag-
glutinin, greatly expanded the information on the assessment of 
chromosomal alterations in human cells (Hsu, 1979). It also became 
feasible to use cytogenetic alterations in human lymphocytes as a 
biodosimeter for assessing human exposures to ionizing radiations 
(Bender and Gooch, 1962).
Two events during the 1970s served to expand the utility of 
mutagenicity data into the realm of risk assessment. The Millers 
and their colleagues (Miller and Miller, 1977) showed that chemi-
cal carcinogens could react to form stable, covalent derivatives with 
DNA, RNA, and proteins both in vitro and in vivo. In addition, they 
reported that these derivatives could require the biotransformation of 
the parent chemical to form reactive metabolites. This metabolism 
is required for some chemicals to become mutagens and carcino-
gens. Metabolic capability is endogenous in vivo, but most in vitro 
cell lines do not express this capacity. To overcome this for in vitro 
mutagenicity studies, Heinrich Malling and colleagues developed 
an exogenous metabolizing system based on a rodent liver homog-
enate (S9) (Malling and Frantz, 1973; Malling, 2004). Although this 
exogenous metabolism system has had utility, it does have draw-
backs related to species and tissue specificity and loss of cellular 
compartmentalization. The development of transgenic cell lines 
containing P450 genes has overcome this drawback to some extent 
(Sawada and Kamataki, 1998; Crespi and Miller, 1999).
The second development in the 1970s that changed the field 
of genetic toxicology was the development by Bruce Ames (Ames 
et al., 1975) of a simple, inexpensive mutation assay with the bac-
terium Salmonella typhimurium. This assay can be used to detect 
chemically induced reverse mutations in several histidine mutant 
strains and can include the exogenous metabolizing S9 system 
described above. The Ames assay, as it is generally called, has 
been expanded and modified to enhance its performance as dis-
cussed below (under section “Gene Mutations in Prokaryotes”). The 
assay has been used extensively, especially for hazard identifica-
tion, as part of the cancer risk assessment process. This use was 
based on the assumption that carcinogens were mutagens and that 
cancer required mutation induction. This latter dogma proved to 
be somewhat inhibitory to the field of genetic toxicology because 
it provided a framework that was too rigid. Nonetheless, over the 
decade of the mid-1970s to mid-1980s somewhere on the order of 
200 short-term genotoxicity and mutagenicity assays were devel-
oped for screening potentially carcinogenic chemicals. The screens 
included mutation induction, chromosomal damage, DNA damage, 
DNA repair, and cell killing or other genotoxic activities.
Several international collaborative studies were organized to 
establish the sensitivity and specificity of a select group of assays

499
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
as well as to assess interlaboratory variation (IPCS, 1988). Most 
assays were able to detect carcinogens or noncarcinogens with an 
efficiency of about 70% as compared with the outcome of two-year 
cancer bioassays (Preston, 1999a). The concordance between stan-
dard battery of in vitro genotoxicity assays and cancer bioassays 
was addressed to some extent by Tennant et al. (1987), who com-
pared the effectiveness of four individual and well-characterized 
short-term assays to identify carcinogens. Again, this battery pre-
dicted about 70% of known carcinogens with the concordance of 
each individual assay was approximately 60%. The authors con-
cluded that no battery of tests improved substantially on the overall 
performance of the Salmonella assay. Subsequently, the lack of a 
tight correlation between carcinogenicity and mutagenicity (and the 
converse, non-carcinogenicity and non-mutagenicity) was found to 
be due to the fact that some chemicals were not directly mutagenic, 
but instead induced changes necessary for tumor development indi-
rectly by, for example, clonally expanding preexisting mutant cells 
(i.e., tumor promotion), inflammation, or through the production 
of reactive oxygen species. Such chemicals are referred to as non-
genotoxic to contrast them with genotoxic mechanism of action. 
In the context of the mechanism of their mutagenicity, it is prefer-
able to distinguish between DNA-reactivity and its correlate non-
DNA-reactivity. Emphasis has recently been placed on identifying 
mechanisms whereby not directly mutagenic chemicals can be 
involved in tumor production. Those identified include cytotoxicity 
with regenerative cell proliferation, mitogenicity, receptor-mediated 
processes, changes in methylation status, immunosuppression, and 
alterations in cell–cell communication.
In the last two decades, the field of genetic toxicology has 
moved away from the short-term assay approach for assessing car-
cinogenicity to a much more mechanistic approach, fueled by the 
advances in molecular biology, analytical techniques, and statisti-
cal methodologies. In addition, genetic toxicology as a field has 
made significant advances from a primarily qualitative assessment 
of hazard without consideration of the dose to a quantitative exami-
nation of the dose–response to facilitate risk assessment (Gollapudi 
et al., 2013; Johnson et al., 2014; Gollapudi, 2017). The ability 
to manipulate and characterize DNA, RNA, and proteins and to 
understand basic cellular processes and how they can be perturbed 
has advanced enormously over this period. Knowing how to take 
advantage of these technical developments is paramount. This 
chapter addresses these changes in approach to genetic toxicology: 
the assays for qualitative and quantitative assessment of cellular 
changes induced by chemical and physical agents, the underlying 
molecular mechanisms for these changes, and how such informa-
tion can be incorporated into cancer and genetic risk assessments.
HEALTH IMPACT OF GENETIC 
ALTERATIONS
Mutations are permanent, transmissible alterations in DNA 
sequences that result in changes to the amount or structure of genetic 
material in the cell. Both small (i.e., one or few base-pair changes 
of a single gene) and large scale (i.e., changes affecting multiple 
genes) DNA alterations can lead to mutations. Mutagenicity refers 
to induction of mutations and the substances that induce mutations 
are referred to as mutagens. Clastogenicity refers to the process of 
inducing chromosomal breaks, and clastogens are the substances 
that induce chromosomal breakage. Clastogenicity can lead to chro-
mosomal deletions and rearrangements and these events can lead to 
mutations provided that they are not lethal to the cell harboring 
them. Substances that induce numerical changes in chromosome 
complement of a cell through loss or gain of whole chromosome(s) 
are referred to as aneugen and these substances induce aneugenic-
ity through changes in the ploidy (i.e., aneuploidy) of the normal 
chromosome complement of the cell. The importance of mutations 
and chromosomal alterations for human health is evident from 
their roles in genetic disorders, including birth defects and cancer. 
Mutations in both germ cells and somatic cells need to be con-
sidered when an overall risk resulting from mutations is assessed. 
However, mutations are also the underlying mechanism of variabil-
ity and adaptation, the basis for evolution.
Somatic Cells
Somatic mutations arise in the genomes of all normal dividing 
and transformed cells as a result of endogenous and exogenous 
processes and stressors (Greenman et al., 2007). An associa-
tion between mutation and cancer has long been evident, such as 
through the correlation between the mutagenicity and carcino-
genicity of chemicals, especially in biological systems that have 
the requisite metabolic activation capabilities. Moreover, human 
chromosome instability syndromes and DNA repair deficiencies 
are associated with increased cancer risk (Friedberg, 1985). Recent 
evidence shows that an average cancer genome contains about 
103 to 104 point mutations, 10 to 102 small insertions or deletions, and 
1 to 10 large-scale chromosome rearrangements (including copy- 
number alterations) (Zhang and Pellman, 2015). Most somatic 
mutations are likely to be “passengers” that do not contribute to 
oncogenesis (Greenman et al., 2007). Overall, approximately 
13,000 unique mutations from 5000 genes and ~340,000 tumors 
have been catalogued with the new next-generation sequencing 
(NGS) techniques (Bell, 2010). However, cancers are specifically 
driven by the accumulation of “driver” mutations in a subset of 
consensus “cancer genes” that confer growth advantage to cells; 
more than 410 mutated genes implicated in cancer development 
have been identified (Futreal et al., 2004; Greenman et al., 2007; 
Stratton et al., 2009; Bell, 2010; Pleasance et al., 2010). “Cancer 
genes” exhibit attributes that confer advantages with the capacity to 
generate their own mitogenic signals, the evasion of apoptosis, the 
resistance of exogenous growth-inhibitory signals, reprogramming 
of energy metabolism, evading immune destruction, cellular prolif-
eration without limits, and acquisition of angiogenic, invasive and 
metastatic properties (Hanahan and Weinberg, 2000; Hanahan and 
Weinberg, 2011); these are classified into 12 signaling pathways 
that regulate three core cellular processes: cell fate, cell survival, 
and genome maintenance (Vogelstein et al., 2013). In turn, these 
characteristics and dysregulated pathways allow cancer pathogen-
esis through genomic instability and mutability that drive tumor 
progression, and elevated inflammatory responses that are tumor-
promoting (Hanahan and Weinberg, 2011).
The advances in the NGS technologies have enabled the com-
prehensive characterization of somatic mutations in large number 
of tumor samples, allowing for new insight and revealing unantici-
pated complexities in the patterns of somatic alterations in cancers 
(Watson et al., 2013). These advances challenge the previously held 
belief of a linear process driven by serially acquired new mutations, 
including base pair substitutions, small insertions and deletions of 
bases, chromosomal rearrangements, and gains and losses in gene 
copy number (Vogelstein and Kinzler, 1993). In a study of 210 
diverse human cancers, more than 1000 somatic base pair mutations 
have been identified along with the evidence for “driver” mutations 
in approximately 120 genes that contribute to the development of 
the studied cancers (Greenman et al., 2007). New NGS evidence 
suggests that most point mutations (including small insertion or

500
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
deletion events) in cancer genomes are passengers that do not con-
tribute to cancer development, and their patterns reflect the underly-
ing mutational processes (Zhang and Pellman, 2015).
New NGS data show that cancer genomes have histopatho-
logically distinct cancer subtypes of the same organ site and often 
have divergent underlying genomic alterations (Watson et al., 
2013). The recent landmark, large-scale cancer genomics analysis, 
applied to almost five million somatic mutations identified in 7042 
cancer samples, has provided insight into the global signatures of 
mutational processes in human cancer (Alexandrov et al., 2013). 
Each of the cancer types had at least two active mutational signa-
tures, while some cancers, including liver and uterus, had up to six 
distinct mutational signatures; mutational signatures include sub-
stitutions, indels, dinucleotide substitutions, kataegis, and strand 
bias. The analysis revealed 27 distinct mutational signatures; 22 of 
these mutational signatures were validated and etiology was pro-
posed for 11 of the 22 validated mutational signatures. Two major 
mutational signatures were associated with age of patient at cancer 
diagnosis and exhibited clear features of C-to-T transitions at CpG 
sites; these major somatic mutations were hypothesized to reflect 
mutations due to normal cellular processes such as deamination of 
5-methylcytosine of CpG sites and DNA replication errors. These 
background mutations were proposed to account for the majority of 
somatic mutations prior to neoplastic development (Alexandrov 
et al., 2013). Two mutational processes were associated with the 
activity of endogenous APOBEC family of deaminases; these 
exhibit predominantly C-to-T and C-to-G mutations at TpC sites 
and have been observed in 17% of all examined cancer samples. 
Distinct mutational signatures were associated with BRCA1/2 
mutations in breast and pancreatic cancers; DNA mismatch repair 
defects in colorectal cancers; hypermutation of the immuno-
globulin genes in chronic lymphocytic leukemia; and recurring 
polymerase ε mutations in uterine and colorectal cancers among 
others. Mutational signatures were also confirmed for ultraviolet 
light exposure as to C-to-T transitions at CpC or TpC sites, and for 
“tobacco smoking signature” as C-to-A transversions.
Cancer cytogenetics have revealed chromosomal alterations, 
including deletions, translocations, inversions, and amplifications, 
and these changes have been implicated in many human ­leukemias 
and lymphomas as well as in some solid tumors (Rabbitts, 1994; 
Zhang et al., 2010). Also, somatic mutations in the noncoding gene 
promoter regions have been recognized as genetic alterations in 
multiple cancer types (Piraino and Furney, 2016). Cancer genomes 
often harbor multiple large-scale chromosomal alterations includ-
ing extensive or whole-chromosome aneuploidy, telomere-bound 
copy-number alterations, chromosomal rearrangements, and com-
plex karyotypes within the same region of chromosomes (Zhang 
and Pellman, 2015). Compared with the analysis of point muta-
tions, the analysis of chromosomal alterations is very preliminary, 
but suggests that chromosomal breakage occurs frequently at 
centromeres and is enriched either in early-replicating, gene-rich 
regions or late-replicating, gene-poor regions, possibly reflecting 
different underlying mechanisms (Zhang and Pellman, 2015). On 
the phenotypic level, the large-scale copy-number alterations and 
chromosomal rearrangements can cause varying changes in fitness 
and are tumor type–dependent. Some aneuploidies are recurrent in 
certain tumor types and almost certainly under positive selection 
(Gordon et al., 2012).
Initial evidence that mutation plays a role in cancer has come 
from molecular studies of acquired somatic mutations in oncogenes, 
or positive growth regulators, and inactivation of tumor-suppressor 
genes. Oncogenes are genes that stimulate the transformation of 
normal cells into cancer cells (Bishop, 1991). They originate when 
genes called proto-oncogenes, involved in normal cellular growth 
and development, are genetically altered. Normal regulation of cel-
lular proliferation requires a balance between factors that promote 
growth and those that restrict it. Mutational alteration of proto-
oncogenes can lead to overexpression of their growth-stimulating 
activity, whereas mutations that inactivate tumor-suppressor genes, 
which normally restrain cellular proliferation, free cells from their 
inhibitory influence (Hanahan and Weinberg, 2000, 2011).
The action of oncogenes is genetically dominant in that a 
single active oncogene is expressed, even though its normal allele 
is present in the same cell. Proto-oncogenes can be converted into 
active oncogenes by point mutations or chromosomal alterations. 
Base pair substitutions in ras proto-oncogenes are found in many 
human tumors (Bishop, 1991; Barrett, 1993; Croce, 2008). Among 
chromosomal alterations that activate proto-oncogenes, transloca-
tions are especially prevalent (Rabbitts, 1994, Croce, 2008; Zhang 
et al., 2010). For example, Burkitt lymphoma involves a transloca-
tion between the long arm of chromosome 8, which is the site of the 
c-MYC oncogene, and chromosome 14 (about 90% of cases), 22, 
or 2. A translocation can activate a proto-oncogene by moving it to 
a new chromosomal location, typically the site of a T-cell recep-
tor or immunoglobulin gene, where its expression is enhanced. A 
similar translocation-based mechanism also applies to various other 
hematopoietic cancers. Alternatively, the translocation may join 
two genes, resulting in a protein fusion that contributes to cancer 
development. Fusions have been implicated in other hematopoietic 
cancers and some solid tumors (Rabbitts, 1994; Croce, 2008; Zhang 
et al., 2010). Like translocations, other chromosomal alterations can 
activate proto-oncogenes, and genetic amplification of oncogenes 
can magnify their expression (Bishop, 1991; Croce, 2008).
Mutational inactivation or deletion of tumor-suppressor genes 
has been implicated in many cancers. Unlike oncogenes, the cancer-
causing alleles that arise from tumor-suppressor genes are typically 
recessive in that they are not expressed when they are heterozy-
gous (Evans and Prosser, 1992). However, several genetic mecha-
nisms, including mutation, deletion, chromosome loss, and mitotic 
recombination, can cause loss of heterozygosity (LOH), in which 
the normal allele is inactivated or eliminated creating a locus with 
no normal gene function. LOH leads to the expression of the reces-
sive cancer gene in a formerly heterozygous cell (Cavenee et al., 
1983; Turner et al., 2003; Reliene et al., 2007). The inactivation of 
tumor-suppressor genes has been associated with various cancers, 
including those of the eye, kidney, colon, brain, breast, lung, and 
bladder (Fearon and Vogelstein, 1990; Marshall, 1991). Mutations 
in a tumor-suppressor gene called P53, located on chromosome 17, 
occur in many different human cancers, and molecular charac-
terization of P53 mutations has linked specific human cancers to 
mutagen exposures (Harris, 1993; Aguilar et al., 1994; Royds and 
Iacopetta, 2006).
In the simplest model for the action of tumor-suppressor 
genes, two events are considered to be required for the development 
of retinoblastoma, a tumor of the eye, because both normal alleles 
must be inactivated or lost (Knudson, 1997). In sporadic forms of 
the cancer without family history of disease, the two genetic events 
occur independently, but in familial cases, such as familial retino-
blastoma, the first mutation is inherited, leaving the need for only a 
single additional event for expression. The strong predisposition to 
cancer in the inherited disease stems from the high likelihood that 
a LOH will occur by mutation, recombination, or aneuploidy in at 
least one or a few cells in the development of the affected organ. 
The simple model involving two events and a single pair of alleles 
cannot explain all observations concerning tumor-suppressor genes 
because many cancers involve more than one tumor-suppressor

501
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
gene. For example, the childhood kidney tumor called Wilms tumor 
can be caused by damage in at least three different genes (Marshall, 
1991), and colorectal carcinomas are often found to have lost not 
only the wild-type P53 tumor-suppressor gene but also other tumor-
suppressor genes (Fearon and Vogelstein, 1990; Stoler et al., 1999). 
Moreover, a single mutation in a tumor-suppressor gene, even 
though not fully expressed, may contribute to carcinogenesis. For 
example, a single P53 mutation in a developing colorectal tumor 
may confer a growth advantage that contributes to the development 
of the disease (Venkatachalam et al., 1998). Subsequent LOH will 
increase the growth advantage as the tumor progresses from benign 
to malignant (Fearon and Vogelstein, 1990). In this regard (mutation 
and selection), carcinogenesis has been likened to an evolutionary 
process, with genomic instability providing the substrate and with 
growth advantage as the selection pressure (Gatenby and Vincent, 
2003; Fischer et al., 2004).
Many cancers involve both activation of oncogenes and inac-
tivation of tumor-suppressor genes (Fearon and Vogelstein, 1990; 
Bishop, 1991; Croce, 2008). The observation of multiple genetic 
changes supports the view that cancer results from an accumula-
tion of genetic alterations and that carcinogenesis is a multistep 
process (Kinzler and Vogelstein, 1996; Hahn et al., 1999; Stoler 
et al., 1999; Croce, 2008). At least three stages have been defined 
in carcinogenesis: initiation, promotion, and progression (Barrett, 
1993). Initiation involves the induction of a genetic alteration, such 
as the mutational activation of a ras proto-oncogene either spon-
taneously or by a mutagen. It is an irreversible step that starts the 
process toward cancer. Promotion involves cellular proliferation 
in an initiated cell population. Promotion can lead to the develop-
ment of benign tumors such as papillomas. Agents called promot-
ers stimulate this process. While some mutagens can also act as 
tumor promoters, most promoters are not mutagens. Promotion is a 
reversible process and generally requires the continuous exposure to 
the promoter. Progression involves the accumulation of additional 
irreversible genetic changes and it is marked by increasing genetic 
instability and malignancy. More recent studies are beginning to 
change this view, leading to the concept of acquired capabilities 
(Hanahan and Weinberg, 2000). In their Hallmarks of Cancer, 
Hanahan and Weinberg (2000) describe a set of six acquired char-
acteristics that are essential for the formation of all tumors irrespec-
tive of tumor type and species. These characteristics are broadly 
described as follows: self-sufficiency in growth signals, insensitiv-
ity to antigrowth signals, evading apoptosis, limitless replicative 
potential, sustained angiogenesis, and tissue invasion and metasta-
sis. It seems probable that there is no specific order for obtaining 
these characteristics. Hanahan and Weinberg have revisited their 
hallmarks concept, and, based on progress in the field over the past 
decade, they have added two emerging hallmarks of potential gener-
ality to their existing six (Hanahan and Weinberg, 2011). These are 
reprogramming of energy metabolism and evading immune destruc-
tion. In addition, they describe genome instability and inflammation 
as general features that underlie their now eight hallmarks (Hanahan 
and Weinberg, 2011).
Genomic instability is a feature of all cancers, with the great 
majority having a large number of chromosomal and gene muta-
tions and aneuploidies. Gene mutations, chromosome aberrations, 
and aneuploidy are all implicated in the development of cancer. 
Mutagens and clastogens contribute to carcinogenesis as initiators. 
Their role does not have to be restricted to initiation, however, in 
that mutagens, clastogens, and aneugens may also contribute to 
the multiple genetic alterations that characterize progression or the 
development of acquired capabilities. Other agents that contrib-
ute to carcinogenesis, such as promoters, need not be mutagens. 
However, the role of mutations is critical, and analyzing mutations 
and mutagenic effects is essential for understanding and predicting 
chemical carcinogenesis. Currently, there is a debate on the fraction 
of cancers arising from random mutations during normal cell divi-
sion versus those mutations contributed by environmental and life-
style factors, with some authors proposing that nearly two-thirds of 
cancer-causing mutations occur spontaneously during cell prolifera-
tion (Tomasetti and Vogelstein, 2017).
Germ Cells
The relevance of gene mutations to health is evident from the many 
disorders that are inherited as simple Mendelian characteristics 
(Mohrenweiser, 1991). About 1.3% of newborns suffer from auto-
somal dominant (1%), autosomal recessive (0.25%), or sex-linked 
(0.05%) genetic diseases (Sankaranarayanan, 1998; Elespuru 
and Sankaranarayanan, 2007; National Research Council [NRC], 
2007a, 2007b). Molecular analysis of the mutations responsible for 
Mendelian diseases has revealed that almost half of these mutations 
are base pair substitutions; of the remainder, most are small dele-
tions (Sankaranarayanan, 1998; Elespuru and Sankaranarayanan, 
2007). In contrast to somatic mutations, common structural vari-
ants in the germ cells have been determined with NGS methods to 
be deletions, tandem duplications, transpositions, inversions, and 
insertions that affect only a small region of the genome and are 
nonoverlapping (Zhang and Pellman, 2015).
Many genetic disorders (such as cystic fibrosis, phenylketon-
uria, Tay-Sachs disease) are caused by the expression of recessive 
mutations. These mutations are mainly inherited from previous gen-
erations and are expressed when an individual inherits the mutant 
gene from both parents. New mutations make a larger contribu-
tion to the incidence of dominant diseases than to that of reces-
sive diseases because only a single dominant mutation is required 
for expression. Thus, new dominant mutations are expressed in 
the first generation. If a dominant disorder is severe, its transmis-
sion between generations is unlikely because of reduced fitness. 
For dominants with a mild effect, reduced penetrance, or a late age 
of onset, the contribution from previous generations is undoubt-
edly larger than it is for mutations with severe early expression. 
Estimating the proportion of all Mendelian genetic diseases that 
can be ascribed to new mutations is not straightforward; a rough 
estimate is 20% (Shelby, 1994).
Besides causing diseases that exhibit Mendelian inheritance, 
gene mutations undoubtedly contribute to human disease through 
the genetic component of disorders with a complex etiology 
(Sankaranarayanan et al., 1999; Sankaranarayanan, 2006). Some 
3% (United Nations Scientific Committee on the Effects of Atomic 
Radiation [UNSCEAR], 2001) or 5% to 6% (Sankaranarayanan, 
1998) of infants are affected by congenital abnormalities; if one 
includes multifactorial disorders that often have a late onset, such 
as heart disease, hypertension, and diabetes, the proportion of the 
population affected increases to more than 60% (Sankaranarayanan, 
1998, 2006; UNSCEAR, 2001). Such frequencies are necessarily 
approximate because of differences among surveys in the report-
ing and classification of disorders. A higher prevalence would 
be found if less severe disorders were included in the tabulation. 
Nevertheless, such estimates provide a sense of the large impact of 
genetic disease.
Sensitive cytogenetic methods have led to the discovery of 
minor variations in chromosome structure that have no apparent 
effect. On the contrary, other relatively minor structural chro-
mosome aberrations cause fetal death or serious abnormalities. 
Aneuploidy (gain or loss of one or more chromosomes) also

502
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
contributes to fetal deaths and causes disorders such as Down syn-
drome. About four infants per 1000 live births have syndromes 
associated with chromosomal abnormalities, including transloca-
tions and aneuploidy. The majority of these syndromes (about 85%) 
result from trisomies (Griffin, 1996; Nagaishi et al., 2004; NRC, 
2007a, 2007b). Most of the adverse effects of chromosomal abnor-
malities occur prenatally. It has been estimated that 5% of all recog-
nized pregnancies involve chromosomal abnormalities, as do about 
6% of infant deaths and 30% of all spontaneous embryonic and 
fetal deaths (Mohrenweiser, 1991; Nagaishi et al., 2004). Among 
the abnormalities that have been observed, aneuploidy is the most 
common, followed by polyploidy. Structural aberrations constitute 
about 5% of the total. Unlike gene mutations, many of which are 
inherited from the previous generation, about 85% of the chromo-
somal anomalies observed in newborns arise de novo in the germ 
cells of the parents (Mohrenweiser, 1991). The frequency of aneu-
ploidy assessed directly in human sperm is 3% to 4%; about 0.4% 
are sex chromosome aneuploidies (Martin et al., 1991, 1996). The 
frequency of aneuploidy in human oocytes is about 18% (Martin 
et al., 1991).
As the field of oncology incorporates NGS approaches into 
tumor and germline analyses, comparisons of tumor to the germ-
line genomes may identify variants associated with susceptibil-
ity to cancer as well as other hereditary diseases (Stadler et al., 
2014). These new advances in NGS, together with identification of 
genetic sequence variants associated with common diseases, have 
the potential to shift the paradigm of personalized medicine, provid-
ing estimates of personal disease risk and the germline genetic risk 
assessment to more precisely guide medical management (Abul-
Husn et al., 2014). For example, meta-analysis of somatic and 
germline DNA sequence biomarker variants of esophageal cancer 
has identified a number of germline polymorphism markers in TP53 
and nucleotide excitation DNA repair pathways associated with 
survival and recurrence following treatment of esophageal cancer 
(Findlay et al., 2015).
CANCER AND GENETIC RISK 
ASSESSMENTS
Cancer Risk Assessment
Genotoxicity and mutagenicity testing is a prerequisite to the assess-
ment of carcinogenic and hereditary risks associated with chemi-
cal exposures (Moolenaar, 1994; EPA, 1996, 2005; ECHA, 2016; 
Eastmond, 2017). Harmonized strategies for mutagenicity testing for 
chemical risk assessment have been developed (Ashby et al., 1996; 
Cimino, 2006; Eastmond et al., 2009; MacGregor et al., 2015a). 
Chemicals are screened in a battery of mutagenicity and genotoxic-
ity assays to identify carcinogenic agents with a mutagenic mode of 
action (MOA). Mutagenic carcinogens share common characteris-
tics and typically produce positive gene mutation and chromosomal 
alteration in multiple in vitro and in vivo test systems (EPA, 2005). 
Mutagenic carcinogens are also active at multi-organ level, in dif-
ferent species, as well as at low-dose exposures across multiple 
doses (Tennant and Spalding, 1996). An adverse outcome pathway 
for mutagenic liver carcinogens was recently proposed involving a 
number of essential key events (KEs) (Becker et al., 2017; Moore 
et al., 2018). The sequence of KEs leading to cancer is initiated 
with a formation of pro-mutagenic DNA adduct/lesion. Following 
insufficient or misrepair of the adduct, a KE of an early mutation in 
critical gene(s) is expected that is to be followed by a KE of cellular 
proliferation and clonal expansion leading to an adverse outcome of 
hepatocellular carcinoma. However, it is important to remember that 
a substance that has induced tumors in animal bioassays is not nec-
essarily acting via a mutagenic MOA (Dearfield and Moore, 2005). 
Moreover, accumulated evidence has demonstrated that somatic 
mutations are brought about by the combination of genetic effects 
and cellular proliferation either by (1) increasing genetic damage 
so there are more errors produced during DNA replication; or (2) 
increasing the rate of DNA replication that increases the likelihood 
that spontaneous errors in DNA become fixed in daughter cells 
(Ames and Gold, 1990; Cohen and Ellwein, 1991; Cohen et  al., 
1991; Cohen and Arnold 2011; Tomasetti and Vogelstein, 2015; 
Tomasetti et al., 2017). Thus, for carcinogen risk assessment, it is 
important to understand impacts not just on genotoxicity, but also 
on cellular proliferation rates, and to distinguish substances that are 
DNA-reactive from substances that act by mechanisms increasing 
cellular proliferation. For substances that are not DNA-reactive, 
mechanisms that can increase cell proliferation include receptor-
mediated responses (e.g., enzyme induction CAR, PXR, AhR) and 
non-receptor mediated MOA (e.g., cytotoxicity leading to regen-
eration) (Becker et al., 2017). These MOAs can also be organized 
into adverse outcome pathway frameworks that provide a basis for 
research, regulatory toxicology testing and risk assessment, among 
other applications such as in eventual regulatory decision-making.
When assessing carcinogenic risks to humans, evaluation 
of complex genotoxicity databases should involve a weight-of-
evidence approach in which studies are critically evaluated based 
on their quality, reproducibility and consistency, significance of 
genetic alternations, phylogenic relevance to humans, type (in vivo 
vs. in vitro), and relevance of the route of chemical administra-
tion (Eastmond, 2017). Using these guidelines, the most weight is 
allocated to high-quality in vivo studies of gene and chromosome 
mutations in humans and mammals exposed via a physiologi-
cally relevant oral, dermal, or inhalation routes (Eastmond, 2017). 
Following evaluation and assessment of the chemical dataset, the 
weight-of-evidence approach is used to reach conclusions about 
chemical’s genotoxicity potential.
The formalized process for conducting a cancer risk assess-
ment has many variations based on national requirements and regu-
lations. Historically in the United States, the default has been linear 
extrapolation from the rodent bioassay tumor data to exposure 
levels consistent with human environmental or occupational expo-
sures (NRC, 1983, 1994; EPA, 1986). The assessment of risk has 
required the application of a series of default approaches for extrap-
olation, for example, from laboratory animals to humans, from high 
to low exposures, from intermittent to chronic lifetime exposures, 
and from route to route of exposure. Subsequently, approaches 
based on mechanistic data were published in Guidelines for 
Carcinogen Risk Assessment to inform the risk assessment process, 
particularly for dose–response assessment and risk characterization 
at low environmental exposures (EPA, 2005). These United States 
Environmental Protection Agency’s (EPA) cancer risk assessment 
guidelines provide a framework that utilizes MOA approach as the 
tool to organize the set of key events (KEs) required for a chemical 
to produce a tumor (Sonich-Mullin et al., 2001; EPA, 2005; Preston 
and Williams, 2005). The EPA guidelines invoke a linear extrapola-
tion and dose–response models in case of mutagenic MOA and as a 
default in case of an absence of sufficient MOA information. MOA 
is a biologically plausible series of chemical-specific key events 
starting with exposure and proceeding through the interaction of 
an agent with a cell, subsequent physiological, tissue, or organ 
changes, and resulting in an adverse effect or outcome (Sonich-
Mullin et al., 2001; Dellarco and Fenner-Crisp, 2012). Furthermore, 
this approach is more consistent with frameworks developed around 
a MOA/human relevance concepts (MOA framework) by the

503
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
World Health Organization/International Programme on Chemical 
Safety (IPCS) (Boobis et al., 2006; Meek et al., 2014a, 2014b) and 
International Life Sciences Institute (ILSI) (Meek et al., 2003).
The IPCS MOA framework has been now widely adopted 
around the world into regulatory guidance (European Food Safety 
Authority, European Chemicals Agency, U.S. EPA, Joint FAO/
WHO Meeting on Pesticide Residues), and hazard characterization/
risk assessment, although variability in its use and conclusions has 
led to different interpretations of the sufficiency of evidence in sup-
port of hypothesized and alternative MOAs (Dourson et al., 2013; 
Becker et al., 2017). The MOA framework is an analytical approach 
where different lines of evidence are organized, linked, and inte-
grated at different levels of biological organization into a more effi-
cient, hypothesis-driven approach to safety evaluation that informs 
a human health risk assessments of chemical exposures (Dellarco 
and Fenner-Crisp, 2012; Meek et al., 2014a; Becker et al., 2017). 
The MOA framework has been extended to enable the evaluation 
for species concordance of effects in the context of environmental 
levels of exposure (Meek et al., 2014b).
The MOA framework continuously evolves to increase 
transparency and defensibility of decisions as well as system-
atic analysis of a weight-of-evidence supporting of all postulated 
chemical MOAs (Becker et al., 2017). The qualitative analysis in 
support of a MOA includes the scoping of potential MOAs and 
consideration of qualitative comparative weight-of-evidence of 
the supporting data. The latest addition to the framework involves 
quantitative weight-of-evidence confidence scoring based on the 
evolved Bradford Hill considerations (Meek et al., 2014a, 2014b; 
Becker et al., 2015, 2017); this latest methodology includes evalu-
ation of the positive, negative, or contradictory studies as well as 
strength of such evidence/counter-evidence that facilitates iden-
tification of the best supported MOA. The evolved Bradford Hill 
considerations for causality evaluate (1) biological concordance; (2) 
essentiality of key events; (3) dose–response concordance, temporal 
concordance, and incidence concordance of empirical observation; 
(4) consistency among different biological contexts; and (5) analogy 
(consistency across structurally significant chemicals) to promote 
better common understanding, consistency in use as well as confi-
dence in particular MOA (Meek et al., 2014b). Lines of evidence 
are evaluated and integrated to elucidate whether a substance acts 
to induce cancer primarily via DNA reactivity or whether it acts by 
mechanisms that increase cell proliferation (e.g., receptor-mediated 
or cytotoxicity leading to regeneration). Once an animal MOA and 
its KEs are established, qualitative and quantitative comparisons 
between experimental animals and humans are made to determine 
whether the MOA is likely operative in humans and, if so, whether 
it can result in a more refined hazard and dose–response assessment 
(Dellarco and Fenner-Crisp, 2012; Meek et al., 2014a, 2014b).
Genetic Risk Assessment
Historically, genetic risk assessment has been interpreted in a quali-
tative, dichotomous “yes” or “no” manner without analysis of dose-
response relationships. It has been recognized that these qualitative 
outcomes of in vitro genotoxicity tests do not adequately predict the 
outcomes of rodent carcinogenicity bioassays or in vivo genotoxic-
ity assays. The traditional “parallelogram approach” has involved 
estimation of the frequency of genetic alterations in human germ 
cells by using rodent germ cell and somatic cell data (Brewen and 
Preston, 1974; Sobels, 1982; Brusick, 1994; Preston et al., 1995; 
UNSCEAR, 2001; Eastmond, 2012, 2017).
New in vitro and in vivo data have demonstrated the existence 
of nonlinear/bilinear dose responses for genotoxic effects, defined 
as a dose–response curve with a slope not significantly different 
from zero gradient below the estimated threshold or Break Point 
Dose (BPD); within the BPD region, no significant difference in 
mutant frequency between the spontaneous background of control 
and the low-dose exposure region following treatment with DNA-
reactive agents (Klapacz et al., 2016; White and Johnson, 2016; 
Wills et al., 2016). Recent international multi-sector efforts have 
been undertaken to improve human health risk assessment for 
DNA-reactive chemicals utilizing a quantitative dose–response 
relationships from in vitro and in vivo genetic toxicology data sets 
to support risk characterization and risk management (Gollapudi 
et al., 2013; Johnson et al., 2014; MacGregor et al., 2015a, 2015b; 
White and Johnson, 2016).
Under the general framework for risk assessment with DNA-
reactive chemicals, the identification of key elements and factors 
should take place such as: (1) hazard assessment; (2) assessment of 
exposure to genotoxicants; (3) methods for dose and effect assess-
ment; (4) risk characterization strategies for genotoxic environmen-
tal chemicals; and (5) monitoring of environmental genotoxicant 
levels (Lewtas et al., 1994; Favor et al., 1995). Under this framework 
presented in Fig. 9-1, the risk assessment can be applicable to most 
situations; furthermore, the risk characterization measures require 
implementation by the regulatory agencies as well as monitoring of 
environmental exposure situations by risk managers (NAS, 1983; 
Lewtas et al., 1994; Favor et al., 1995; MacGregor et al., 2015a).
This framework focuses on minimization of the genotoxic risk 
and protecting against the risk of developing diseases (MacGregor 
et al., 2015a). A number of quantitative approaches for analyzing 
dose–response curves for genotoxic effects and deriving point-of-
departure (PoD) metrics have been evaluated that are based on the 
research data; these PoD metrics are: the no-observed-genotoxic-
effect-level (NOGEL) or the highest dose tested for which there 
is no significant increase in genotoxic effect compared to control; 
the threshold effect level (BPD or Td) or the dose at which the 
slope changes from zero (horizontal) to positive, with its standard 
error forming the confidence bounds (90% CI); the benchmark 
dose (BMD10) or the dose that produces 10% increase over the fit-
ted background; BMD1SD (the change in response corresponding to 
one standard deviation) or 10% excess risk for individuals below 
and above the 2nd and 98th percentiles, respectively; and slope-
transition-dose (STD) or the lowest dose for which the lower bound 
of the 95% confidence interval of the slope exceeds zero (Gollapudi 
et al., 2013; Johnson et al., 2014).
The PoD descriptors were determined for a number of chemi-
cals, including the data-rich, prototypical SN1 and SN2-type alkylat-
ing agents (Gocke and Muller, 2009; Gollapudi et al., 2013; Cao 
et al., 2014; Johnson et al., 2014; Ji et al., 2016). NOGEL, BMDL10, 
and BMDL1SD PoD metrics can be readily calculated from most 
gene mutation and chromosomal damage studies and the BMDL10 
values were often lower than the other PoDs (Johnson et al., 2014; 
MacGregor et al., 2015a). Analysis of obtained PoD values showed 
that these different approaches are complementary with respect 
to their advantages and limitations; have values within the same 
order of magnitude; and demonstrate more variability for the in 
vivo assays. The BMDL10 is considered a satisfactory PoD matrix 
for the analysis of genotoxic effects, as it typically yields a good 
fit to the data, generally provides the most conservative value, is 
flexible, and can be readily applied to a wide range of datasets. 
It should be noted that many DNA-reactive chemicals have been 
demonstrated to exhibit nonlinear/bilinear dose responses for 
genotoxic effects, such as formaldehyde, vinyl acetate, and pro-
pylene oxide (Swenberg et al., 1983; Albertini and Sweeney, 2007; 
Albertini, 2013).

504
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
Once the genotoxic PoD values are obtained from in vivo test 
systems or target tissues with relevance to human health, appro-
priate extrapolation method(s) and uncertainty/assessment/safety 
factors need to be applied for the risk assessment. The choice of 
appropriate methods and assessment factors depends on knowledge 
of the mechanisms behind the dose–response relationships, the 
critical MOA information for DNA-reactive agents, and the entire 
weigh-of-evidence of the toxicological dataset (MacGregor et al., 
2015b). The mechanistic information used by authoritative bodies 
in their risk assessment has included: critical involvement of non-
DNA targets; contribution of DNA repair mechanisms; exceeded 
detoxification capacity; disruption of enzymes involved in DNA 
synthesis or replication; chemical reactivity or properties unlikely to 
occur in vivo; inadequate uptake or toxicokinetics limiting distribu-
tion to target; mutational spectrum in tumor genes similar to those in 
untreated animals; structural similarities to similar threshold-acting 
chemicals; secondary or indirect origin of the observed damage; and 
species and tumor-specific non-genotoxic MOA (MacGregor et al., 
2015b). Parameters and safety factors that need to be evaluated in 
order to minimize the uncertainty in the assessment include: (a) spe-
cies differences and allometric scaling; (b) differences in absorption, 
distribution, metabolism, and pharmacokinetics; (c) differences in 
duration of exposure; (d) severity of toxicity endpoint; (e) variabil-
ity among individuals; and (f) uncertainty in PoD (MacGregor et al., 
2015b). When data that permit assessment of these parameters/ 
factors are not available, default adjustment factors of up to 10,000-
fold can be applied to extrapolate to the acceptable human exposure 
levels. Knowledge of exposure, metabolism, and pharmacokinetics 
can help refine this extrapolation and reduce the assessment factor 
values by replacement with the experimentally determined param-
eters. Knowledge of a particular DNA adduct formed, its stability, 
its pro-mutagenic potential, its miscoding properties, the efficiency 
and fidelity of DNA repair in a particular tissue or cell type, and the 
replication rates of tissue or cell types can be used as a surrogate 
marker of a biologically effective dose (MacGregor et al., 2015b).
Overall, the derived genotoxic PoD values can be combined 
with appropriate assessment factors for the purpose of conduct-
ing the quantitative risk assessment for DNA-reactive agents. This 
approach will facilitate the derivation of reference doses below 
which there are no additional significant risk to human health as 
well as margin of exposure values useful for evaluating human 
health risk and regulatory decision making.
FORMATION OF DNA DAMAGE AND DNA 
DAMAGE RESPONSE PATHWAYS
DNA Damage
Preservation of genome integrity is central to cellular survival and 
successful propagation of hereditary material, yet all organisms 
sustain a certain number of background mutations as a result of 
limited DNA chemical stability, numerous stressors, cellular pro-
cesses, and interactions with the environment (Lindahl, 1993). 
Endogenous and exogenous chemicals produce a wide array of 
DNA lesions such as covalent DNA base adducts, cross-links 
between DNA bases or between DNA bases and proteins, ­and 
single- and double-strand breaks. DNA replication, transcription, 
or erroneous incorporation of ribonucleotides into DNA can also be 
a source of spontaneous mutation and genome instability (Echols 
and Goodman, 1991; Datta and Jinks-Robertson, 1995; McElhinny 
et  al., 2010). Ubiquitously present ultraviolet radiation, ionizing 
radiation, and exogenous hydrocarbons also present threats to 
genomic stability (Fig. 9-2).
In order to maintain the genome integrity and to assure accu-
rate duplication of genomic information by DNA and RNA poly-
merases, this damage must be faithfully repaired and/or addressed 
by posttranslational activation of cell cycle networks and tran-
scriptional networks for apoptosis/cell death (Guerard et al., 2015; 
Thomas et al., 2015; Klapacz et al., 2016). The understanding of the 
cellular mechanisms acting within the background mutation regions 
of the genotoxic dose–response curve can facilitate better risk char-
acterization from exposures to DNA-reactive chemicals, the deter-
mination of points of departure (PoDs) metrics for genotoxic effects 
that minimize genotoxic risk and protect against disease develop-
ment (Gollapudi et al., 2013; Johnson et al., 2014; MacGregor et al., 
2015a, 2015b; Klapacz et al., 2016). The aim of this section is to 
introduce the topic of DNA damage because such damage is the 
molecular initiating event for the formation of genetic alterations

and genotoxicity in general. For an expressed mutation, several key 
events must occur downstream from the initial DNA adduct forma-
tion, including insufficient adduct repair, DNA replication, and cell 
division (Pottenger and Gollapudi, 2010). Much greater detail can 
be found in recent reviews that are referenced within this section.
Background DNA Damage  Genomes sustain a continuous 
damage load due to numerous stressors, cellular metabolism, 
and limited chemical stability of DNA (Lindahl, 1993, 2000). 
The electrophilic by-products of endogenous metabolism include 
reactive oxygen species formed as by-products of cellular oxygen 
consumption (such as superoxide O2
•, hydroxyl free radicals ·OH, 
and hydrogen peroxide), nitric oxide and its metabolites causing 
nitrosative stress and pro-inflammatory responses, lipid peroxida-
tion by-products, aldehydes, and alkylators. Endogenous processes 
produce oxidized and alkylated bases, single- and double-strand 
breaks, interstrand cross-links, hydrolytic and enzymatic deamina-
tion of cytosine and 5-methylcytosine leading to uracil and thy-
mine, respectively. In the absence of any significant exogenous 
exposures, the steady-state background DNA damage load was 
estimated at ≥50,000 lesions per cell (Swenberg et al., 2011). 
The spontaneous daily damage load includes ~30,000 noninstruc-
tional and pro-mutagenic abasic sites resulting from spontaneous 
purine losses or enzymatic adduct/base cleavage, alkylated (N7-
methyl-dG) and oxidized (8-oxo-dG) bases, formaldehyde adducts, 
acrolein-dG adducts, and various etheno-purines (Lindahl, 1993; 
Swenberg et al., 2011). This significant and ubiquitous background 
of pro-mutagenic DNA background damage is similar to that 
caused by exogenous DNA-reactive agents and is likely causative 
for the normal range of background mutations (Swenberg et al., 
2011; Sharma et al., 2014).
The process of DNA replication itself is error-prone as one in 
about 106 replicated bases can be mis-inserted by replicative poly-
merases, or the polymerase can slip at repetitive sequences resulting 
in as small insertion–deletion loops (IDLs). However, the frequency 
of mis-inserted bases in replication is not the sole determinant of 
the spontaneous mutation frequency. Living cells reduce the rate of 
polymerase error appreciably through error recognition and repair 
processes (Johnson, 2010). A high-fidelity polymerase may make 
one error in a million bases while copying roughly 300 base pairs 
per second, but polymerase proofreading and mismatch correction 
then lower the error rate as much as 1000-fold (Johnson, 2010). The 
evolutionarily conserved mismatch repair pathway further corrects 
base pair mismatches as well as IDLs that escape the proofreading 
activity of DNA polymerases (Modrich and Lahue, 1996; Schofield 
and Hsieh, 2003); mismatch repair contributes about 1000-fold to 
the overall DNA replication fidelity (Jaworski et al., 1995; Jiricny, 
2006). The result is a low spontaneous mutation rate of the DNA 
replication process. A recent study based on the sequencing of 19 
Escherichia coli genomes in a 40,000-generation experiment esti-
mated the rate of spontaneous base pair substitutions to be 8.9 × 
10−11 per base pair per generation (Wielgoss et al., 2011). Finally, 
high levels of transcription in all organisms stimulate a wide vari-
ety of mutation types that reflect multiple molecular mechanisms 
(Jinks-Robertson and Bhagwat, 2014).
Ionizing Radiation  Electromagnetic waves that carry enough 
energy to ionize or remove electrons from an atom are defined as 
ionizing radiation (IR). Major exposure sources of general popula-
tions to IR include background waves, nuclear medicine, occupa-
tional exposure, industrial and consumer sources (NCRP, 2008). 
X-rays, γ-rays, neutrons, and α and β particles are types of IR that 
can produce radiochemical DNA damage either by direct or indirect 
mechanisms causing single- and double-strand breaks, abasic sites 
and a broad range of oxidative base damages from indirect creation 
of free radicals (Goodhead, 1994; Wallace, 1994; Ward, 1994; Cadet 
et al., 2011). The signature of IR exposure is the induction of DNA 
clustered damaged sites, defined as two or more lesions within one 
to two helical turns of DNA (Eccles et al., 2011). Clustered dam-
age can either be associated with double-strand breaks, oxidized 
base lesions or abasic sites or with single-strand breaks, oxidized 
base lesions, abasic sites. These multiple lesions can be formed in 
DNA from the same radiation energy deposition event (Blaisdell 
et al., 2001). The relative proportions of these different classes of 
DNA damage vary with type of radiation. For example, single-
strand breaks and base damages predominate with x-rays, for

which ionization density is sparse, whereas increased frequencies 
of single- and double-strand breaks occur as a result of exposure to 
α particles for which ionization is dense. The 7,8-dihydro-8-oxo-
2′-deoxyguanosine (8-oxo-dG) base damage is considered as a bio-
marker for IR exposure and radiosensitivity (Erhola et al., 1997; 
Roszkowski and Olinski, 2012). Other base lesions such as thymine 
glycols, hypoxanthine, 1,N6-ethenoadenine, or 3,N4-ethenocytosine 
were also quantified following IR exposure (Le et al., 1998; Olipitz 
et al., 2012). It has been demonstrated that the modified histone 
γ-H2AX can be used as a sensitive biomarker of IR exposure fol-
lowing formation of DNA double-strand breaks (Nakamura et al., 
2006; Mah et al., 2010; Shee et al., 2013). The alkaline comet assay 
is also routinely used to measure DNA strand break damage fol-
lowing patient exposure to IR (Alapetite et al., 1999; Dusinska and 
Collins, 2008; Borrego-Soto et al., 2015).
Low-dose ionizing radiation is known to induce radioadap-
tive responses in vitro as well as in mice in vivo. The importance 
of dose-rate as the critical determinant of a genotoxic and adverse 
outcome has been demonstrated and presumably reflects saturation 
of the DNA repair and damage response capabilities at high-dose 
exposures leading to adverse outcomes including mutation. No 
accumulation of DNA damage such as base lesions and micro-
nuclei, sequence rearrangements, and gene expression of several 
radiation-sensitive genes following low-dose subacute exposure to 
10.5 cGy γ-radiation was seen in mice (Olipitz et al., 2012). This 
IR dose corresponds to approximately 400-fold increase over back-
ground dose, inducing formation of additional ~400 base lesions 
per cell. Meanwhile, when the same total amount of radiation was 
delivered in one acute dose, DNA double-strand breaks and DNA 
stress responses were detected.
Ultraviolet Light  Ultraviolet (UV) light is a type of electro-
magnetic nonionizing radiation with the wavelength range shorter 
than that of the violet end of the visible spectrum, but longer than 
that of x-rays. Our bodies use UV light to make vitamin D, but 
DNA is certainly one of its key ubiquitous targets; high UV expo-
sure causes skin burns and can even lead to cancer. UV radiation 
induces two of the most predominant and cytotoxic DNA lesions, 
cyclobutane-pyrimidine dimers (CPDs) and 6-4 photoproducts 
(6-4PPs) as well as their Dewar valence isomers. These lesions 
and biological responses to these lesions in various organisms have 
been well characterized and are described below (Friedberg et al., 
1995). Organisms have developed a number of repair and toler-
ance mechanisms to counteract UV-induced DNA damage (Sinha 
and Häder, 2002). Photoreactivation with the help of the enzyme 
photolyase is one of the most important and frequently occurring 
repair mechanisms in a variety of microorganisms. Excision repair 
plays an important role in eukaryotic DNA repair of UV-induced 
damage. In addition, mechanisms such as mutagenic repair, dimer 
translesion bypass, homologous recombination repair, cell-cycle 
checkpoints, apoptosis, and certain alternative repair pathways are 
also operative in various organisms to counteract consequences of 
UV exposures.
Chemical Agents  The major classes of DNA-reactive chemicals 
include polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons, arylamines and nitro-
aryl hydrocarbons, aflatoxins, small alkylators, oxidating, reducing 
and deaminating chemicals, halogenated hydrocarbons, DNA-DNA 
and DNA-protein cross-linkers, strand breakers, and DNA interca-
lators; these are presented in Figure 9-2. Chemicals can produce 
DNA alterations either directly by DNA-reactive mechanisms of 
adduct formation or indirectly by intercalation of polycyclic, aro-
matic, and planar chemicals between the base pairs (Heflich, 1991). 
The conversion of DNA adducts or other structural modifications 
into gene and chromosomal mutations by errors of DNA replication 
on the damaged template, or occasionally by errors of DNA repair, 
can lead to cells being initiated along the key events pathway to the 
adverse outcome of cancer.
Many electrophilic chemicals react with nucleophilic sites 
within DNA, forming covalent addition products called biomarker 
DNA adducts, or some other DNA structural modifications. The 
DNA bases involved and the positions on DNA bases can be spe-
cific for a given chemical; base pairing can protect certain base 
positions from the adduction. Such specificity of DNA damage can 
result in a spectrum of mutations that is chemical-specific (Dogliotti 
et al., 1998; Jarabek et al., 2009). Some alkylated bases can mispair, 
causing mutations when DNA is replicated, other adducts are cyto-
toxic, while others can be both mutagenic and cytotoxic; impor-
tantly the most common alkylating adduct at N7 position of dG is 
not mutagenic. Ubiquitous alkyl adducts at the N3 and N7 positions 
of purines destabilize the N-glycosidic bond between the base and 
sugar backbone making these modified bases more susceptible to 
depurination into abasic sites (Wyatt and Pittman, 2006; Swenberg 
et al., 2011). Abasic sites are noninstructional and pro-mutagenic 
as the insertion of incorrect bases across from the abasic sites can 
cause mutations (Laval et al., 1990). The most abundant alkyl 
adduct produced by alkylating agents is at the N7 position of gua-
nine, a site that has the highest negative electrostatic potential in 
DNA (Pullman and Pullman, 1981; Beranek, 1990). However, N7 
C1-C3 alkyl guanine adducts are not considered to be cytotoxic 
or mutagenic as they do not block DNA replication and are well 
tolerated in the cells (Boiteux and Laval, 1983; Larson et al., 1985; 
Boysen et al., 2009; Philippin et al., 2014).
Bulky DNA adducts that distort DNA double helix, such as cis-
platin, metabolites of benzo(a)pyrene, or N-2-acetylaminofluorene, 
are recognized and repaired by excision repair in a similar way 
to UV damages (Gillet and Scharer, 2006). These bulky adducts 
impede progress of DNA and RNA polymerases, causing repli-
cation fork collapse or stalled transcription, respectively. If unre-
paired, these lesions can be bypassed during DNA replication in 
an error-prone or error-free manner by translesion synthesis (TLS).
Indirect intercalators such as acridine compounds (9-amino-
acridine, ethidium bromide, etc.) and other planar molecules such 
as daunomycin, doxorubicin, and thalidomide in repetitive DNA 
sequences have been associated with the induction of frameshift 
mutations in which one or two base pairs have been gained or lost 
(Ferguson and Denny, 1990; Hoffmann et al., 2003).
DNA Repair and Tolerance Pathways
The thousands of the steady-state background DNA lesions are readily 
repaired in cells by an integrated network of DNA repair pathways 
that includes direct repair (DR), base excision repair (BER), nucleotide 
excision repair (NER), mismatch repair (MMR), nonhomologous end- 
joining of double-strand breaks (NHEJ), homologous repair pathway 
(HR), and Fanconi Anemia (FA) pathway; these are presented in 
Figure 9-2. Among these repair activities, base excision repair is con-
sidered one of the most active, handling thousands of abasic sites, 
alkylated, oxidized and deaminated bases, single-strand breaks and 
nicks per day. These pathways also have overlapping repair substrates; 
for example, the most predominant abasic sites are also processed by 
homologous recombination between two similar or identical DNA 
sequences, nucleotide excision repair, or translesion synthesis that 
allows the DNA replication past unrepaired DNA lesions (Boiteux 
and Guillet 2004; Covo et al., 2004; Adar et al., 2009). The basic 
principles underlying DNA repair processes involve damage rec-
ognition, followed by either direct reversal of the damage (sealing

507
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
of strand breaks or removal of alkyl adducts from DNA bases) or 
excision of damage that involves sequential steps of repair DNA syn-
thesis, and ligation of strands. DNA repair is usually error-free, but 
there may be rare events where the misrepair will result in genotoxic 
outcomes (Wiktor-Brown et al., 2008; Sukup-Jackson et al., 2014; 
Klapacz et al., 2016). DNA repair influences the genotoxic outcome, 
the dose–response of mutation and chromosome damage following 
exposure to DNA-reactive agents at all exposure levels (Kirsch-
Volders et al., 2000; Parry et al., 2000; Guerard et al., 2015; Thomas 
et al., 2015).
In contrast, if the sustained damage is extensive, the cell can 
undergo apoptosis (programmed cell death), effectively avoiding it 
becoming a mutant cell (Evan and Littlewood, 1998). If the damage 
is less severe, it can be repaired by a range of processes that are a 
part of generalized and complex cellular response networks; these 
processes can return the damaged DNA to its undamaged state by 
error-free post-replication damage avoidance pathway utilizing HR, 
or to an improved, but still altered, state involving the Radiation 
Sensitivity Proteins RAD6 and RAD18 protein complex respon-
sible for Proliferating Cell Nuclear Antigen (PCNA) modification 
and activation of translesion synthesis (TLS) (Hoege et al., 2002; 
McIntyre et al., 2006). During TLS, a family of prokaryotic and 
eukaryotic TLS polymerases, such as the human Y-family polymer-
ases eta, iota, kappa, and Rev1, can bypass lesions that otherwise 
would block replication by the normal processive DNA polymer-
ases (Rattray and Strathern, 2003; Prakash et al., 2005). These con-
served polymerases have the ability to bypass specific DNA lesions 
or groups of lesions. The result of the lesion bypass can be an inser-
tion of incorrect dNTP or a correct one depending on the induced 
lesion and the particular bypass polymerase.
This chapter presents a brief outline of the major classes of DNA 
repair and tolerance pathways (Fig. 9-3); much greater detail can be 
found in the reviews provided for each section and general reviews 
by Van Houten and Albertini (1995), Friedberg (2000), Krokan et al. 
(2000), Wood et al. (2005), Bansbach and Cortez (2011), Chatterjee 
and Siede (2013), and Coleman and Huang (2016).
Base Excision Repair (BER)  The base excision repair (BER) 
pathway is one of the most active DNA repair pathways in cells 
operating at all cell cycle stages. BER repairs non-bulky oxida-
tive, deaminated, and alkylated modifications to bases or the sugar 
phosphate backbone as well as abasic sites both in the nucleus and 
mitochondria (Chatterjee and Walker, 2017). BER involves five key 
enzymatic steps (Fig. 9-3A) to remove the initial DNA lesion and 
restore the original sequence, starting with (i) lesion recognition and 
excision of damaged base or inappropriate base by N-glycosylase; 
(ii) incision of the phosphodiester backbone at the resulting aba-
sic site by apurinic/apyrimidinic (AP) endonuclease; (iii) termini 
clean-up to permit repair synthesis and/or nick ligation; (iv) gap-
filling to replace the excised nucleotide by DNA polymerase; and 
(v) sealing of the final nick by DNA ligase enzymes.
BER is initiated by a class of lesion-specific enzymes called 
DNA glycosylases that recognize and cleave the damaged base to an 
abasic site (Lindahl, 1976; Demple and Harrison, 1994; Seeberg et 
al., 1995; Wood, 1996; McCullough et al., 1999; Sung and Demple, 
2006); at least 11 different DNA glycosylases have been identified. 
Glycosylases are known to share overlapping substrates; for exam-
ple, 8-oxoguanine glycosylase (OGG1), nei endonuclease VIII-like 
NEIL1, and NEIL3 glycosylases can all process 8-oxo-dG. DNA 
glycosylases initiate BER by either short-patch (one nucleotide) or 
long-patch (often 6 to 13 nucleotides) BER. The abasic sites formed 
by DNA glycosylases are recognized and processed by AP endo-
nucleases, such as APE1 or Apn1 to create incision of the phos-
phodiester backbone (Wood et al., 2001). DNA synthesis to fill the 
repair patch is completed by DNA polymerases, such as Pol β for 
short-patch repair, and processive Pol δ or Pol ε polymerases for 
long-patch repair. Two additional strand incision repair pathways 
that feed into long-patch BER have been characterized: nucleotide 
incision repair (NIR) where AP endonuclease directly incises 5′ 
to oxidized and alkylated pyrimidines (Ischenko and Saparbaev, 
2002); and ribonucleotide excision repair (RER) initiated by the 
RNase H2 complex that incises the strand 5′ to the misincorporated 
ribonucleotide (Sparks et al., 2012). Finally, repair of direct or indi-
rect single stranded DNA breaks is undertaken by the single-strand 
break repair (SSBR) pathway that feeds into BER. SSBR is divided 
into four different processes beginning with detection of a break, 
followed by DNA end processing, gap-filling, and finally ligation 
of the backbone (Fan and Wilson 2005). The importance of the BER 
pathway is highlighted by the fact that deficiencies in the core BER 
enzymes are incompatible with life and result in embryonic lethal-
ity and cell death (Xanthoudakis et al., 1996; Tebbs et al., 1999; 
Fung and Demple, 2005). Defects in BER enzymes lead to reduced

cell survival, elevated mutation rates, DNA-damaging agent hyper-
