Nucleotide Excision Repair (NER)  The nucleotide excision 
repair (NER) pathway is a highly conserved pathway that recog-
nizes and removes bulky DNA lesions that distort DNA double 
helix and impede progress of DNA and RNA polymerases, caus-
ing replication fork collapse or stalled transcription, respectively 
(Lommel et al., 1995; Gillet and Scharer, 2006; Jiang and Sancar, 
2006). Substrates for NER include UV-induced lesions; cispla-
tin and hydrocarbon base adducts; chemicals in tobacco smoke, 
aflatoxin B1, endogenous lipid peroxidation products; and ROS-
induced base modifications (cyclopurines). NER uses about 30 
proteins to remove the damage-containing 25 to 30 base pairs long 
oligonucleotide from DNA (Reardon and Sancar, 2005; Reed, 
2011). The basic four steps of NER involve: (i) damage recognition 
and incision on both sides of the lesion, (ii) excision, (iii) repair 
synthesis, and (iv) ligation of the nicks (Fig. 9-3A). If unrepaired, 
these lesions can be bypassed in an error-prone manner by TLS 
polymerases. The characterization of these steps has been facili-
tated by the use of rodent mutant cell lines and cells from indi-
viduals with syndromes characterized by the UV-hypersensitivity, 
neurodevelopmental abnormalities, and skin cancers (Broughton 
et al., 2001). The defects in NER pathway are genetically linked to 
a group of autosomal recessive human diseases such as xeroderma 
pigmentosum (XP), Cockayne syndrome (CS), and photosensitive 
form of trichothiodystrophy. The cardinal feature of such patients is 
an extremely high incidence of UV-related cancers.
NER consists of a general pathway termed global genomic 
repair (GGR) that removes lesions from the entire genome and a spe-
cialized pathway referred to as transcription-coupled repair (TCR). 
These pathways differ in the mechanism of damage recognition, but 
share the same mechanism for removal and repair of the damage. 
In GGR, the xeroderma pigmentosum group C-complementing, or 
XPC complex, is the initial damage recognition factor that recog-
nizes the damage-induced structural change in DNA, binds to the 
strand opposite the adduct, mediates the recruitment of transcrip-
tion factor II human (TFIIH) complex, and initiates excision repair 
(Maillard et al., 2007; Min and Pavletich, 2007; Scharer, 2007). In 
TCR, stalled RNA polymerase II (RNAP) serves as the damage rec-
ognition step (Mellon and Hanawalt, 1989). In both pathways, TFIIH 
complex, containing XP type B and D, XPB (3′-5′) and XPD (5′-3′) 
helicases respectively, promotes the opening of DNA duplex around 
the adduct (Coin et al., 2007; Sugasawa et al., 2009; Mathieu et al., 
2010). XPF-ERCC1 (for XP type F and excision repair cross com-
plementation group 1) and XP type G (XPG) endonucleases are then 
responsible for dual incision 5′ and 3′ to the DNA damage, respec-
tively (Evans et al., 1997). After the dual incision event and removal 
of the damage-containing oligonucleotide fragment, DNA Pols δ, ε, 
or κ carry out gap-filling synthesis in cooperation with replication 
factor C (RFC) and proliferating cell nuclear antigen (PCNA) (Tang 
and Chu, 2002; Mocquet et al., 2008; Ogi et al., 2010). Finally, the 
nick is sealed in by ligase I or III complexes (Aboussekhra et al., 
1995; Araújo et al., 2000; Moser et al., 2007; Mocquet et al., 2008).
Double-Strand Break Repair  Cell survival and genome integ-
rity are seriously compromised by the presence of broken chro-
mosomes. Despite these consequences, double-strand breaks are 
exploited by cells to control a number of biological processes such 
as initiation of V(D)J recombination during antibody diversification, 
recombination of genetic material during meiosis, and decatenation 
of DNA molecules by topoisomerase II (Helleday et al., 2007). 
When double-strand breaks occur, the ends of the DNA are mostly 
not directly ligatable. To cope with the damage, all organisms have 
evolved several double-strand break repair mechanisms, of which 
NHEJ and HR are the most prominent (Shinohara and Ogawa, 
1995; Critchlow and Jackson, 1998; Haber, 2000; Sonoda et al., 
2006; Mladenov and Iliakis, 2011). Alternative error-prone double-
strand break repair pathways, namely alternative end joining (alt-
EJ) and single-strand annealing (SSA), have been recognized to 
compete for repair with classical HR (Fig. 9-3C) (Decottignies, 
2013; Ceccaldi et al., 2015). These pathways are largely similar 
across a broad range of species from yeast to humans, although 
the most frequently utilized one differ among species. Unrepaired 
double-strand breaks activate DNA damage repair (DDR) networks 
that allow either recognition of the break, activation of cellular 
checkpoints and DNA repair, or induction of apoptosis (Misteli and 
Soutoglou, 2009). Chromatin plays a crucial role in the regulation 
of DDR and double-strand break repair (Lemaître and Soutoglou, 
2014) with phosphorylated histone H2AX (γH2AX) serving as an 
early response following double-strand break formation (Rogakou 
et al., 1998, 1999). Formed γH2AX/MCD1 complex then either 
recruits p53 binding protein 1 (53BP1) to direct repair toward 
NHEJ, or BRCA1 to direct repair toward HR. MRN complex and 
ATM participate in both HR and NHEJ (Paull and Gellert, 1998, 
2000). Recent evidence also suggests that small non-protein-­coding 
RNAs are generated at the sites of DNA damage and control DDR 
network responses (d’Adda di Fagagna, 2014).
Homologous Recombination (HR)  Eukaryotes undergo homol-
ogous recombination (HR) between similar or identical DNA 
sequences as part of their normal activities both in germ cells 
(meiotic recombination) and somatic cells (mitotic recombination) 
(Zheng et al., 2011). In eukaryotes, the process has been charac-
terized most extensively for yeast, and these processes have been 
largely conserved in higher eukaryotes (Johnson et al., 1999; Krejci 
et al., 2003; West, 2003; Cahill et al., 2006). HR is the principal 
mode of double-strand break repair in the S and G2 phases of the 
cell cycle and is facilitated by the proximity of a sister chromatid 
with essentially the identical sequence as the broken chromosome 
(Fig. 9-3C). HR has been proposed to be initiated by the recognition 
of double-strand break by the Mre11-Rad50-Nbs1 (MRN) complex 
that acts as a break sensor and recruits ataxia-telangiectasia mutated 
(ATM) kinase to the site of damage (Stracker and Petrini, 2011). 
The initiating step involves the processing of the DNA ends imme-
diately adjacent to the double-strand break and the production of a 
3′-single-stranded tail by exonucleases or helicase activity. This is 
followed by the location of regions of homology, strand invasion 
with D-loop formation, and branch migration whereby the single-
stranded tail invades an undamaged homologous DNA molecule 
and Holliday junction DNA complex is formed; Holliday junction 
is cross-shaped structure that forms when two double-stranded 
DNA molecules become separated into four strands in order to 
exchange segments of genetic information. DNA synthesis and 
the resolution of the Holliday junction by its cleavage produce two 
DNA molecules (with or without a structural crossover). Additional 
models have also been proposed (Haber, 2000; Jasin and Rothstein, 
2013). A detailed description of the specific enzymes known to 
be involved can be found in Shinohara and Ogawa (1995), Cahill 
et al. (2006), Helleday et al. (2007), Hiom (2010), and Jasin and 
Rothstein (2013).
Nonhomologous End-Joining (NHEJ)  Classical nonhomolo-
gous end-joining (NHEJ) pathway joins two broken DNA ends in 
close spatial proximity to each other by direct re-ligation (Lieber

509
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
et al., 2003). NHEJ does not require extensive sequence homology 
or genetic recombination and the repair is influenced by regions 
of microhomology of to 14 base pairs. NHEJ is the major double- 
strand break repair pathway in higher eukaryotes (Burma et al, 
2006). NHEJ can occur throughout cell cycle, but it predominates 
in the G0/G1 and G2 phases (Lieber, 2010). NHEJ is also an inte-
gral part of the V(D)J recombination, generating the vast antibody 
diversity and T-cell receptor proteins, while ensuring genomic sta-
bility (Malu et al., 2012).
NHEJ entails three main steps: recognition of the two-ended 
strand breaks; processing to remove non-ligatable termini; and join-
ing of the two ends. To initiate NHEJ, the Ku70/Ku80 heterodimer 
binds directly to the two broken ends, and recruits the DNA-
dependent protein kinase DNA-PKcs to facilitate the alignment of 
the broken ends (Mimori et al., 1986; Cary et al., 1997; Ramsden 
and Gellert, 1998). DNA-PKcs activates DNA endonucleases that 
trim the 3′ and 5′ single-stranded overhangs at the DNA ends to 
reveal complementary nucleotide stretches (Ma et al., 2002; Neal 
and Meek, 2011). This processing often results in nucleotide loss. 
In instances where short nucleotide gaps remain after microhomol-
ogy-mediated annealing, DNA Pols µ and λ fill in the small gap 
segments (Mahajan et al., 2002; Fan and Wu, 2004). Finally, ligase 
IV complex stimulates end joining to complete the repair process 
(Ahnesorg et al., 2006).
Fanconi Anemia (FA) Pathway  Fanconi anemia (FA) DNA 
excision repair pathway is involved in the repair of interstrand 
cross-links (ICLs) produced by cross-linking chemicals such as 
cisplatin, diepoxybutane, and mitomycin C (Coleman and Huang, 
2016). ICLs can interfere with cellular processes requiring separa-
tion of the two DNA strands, such as DNA replication and tran-
scription. Unrepaired ICLs can lead to mutations, chromosome 
breakage, and mitotic catastrophe. In addition to recognition and 
repair of ICLs, the complex FA pathway orchestrates cooperation 
of other DNA repair processes to complete ICLs repair process, 
such as HR, NER, and TLS. Individuals with FA protein defects 
exhibit hypersensitivity to cross-linking agents, developmental 
defects, bone marrow failure, and cancer predisposition.
Mismatch Repair (MMR)  The principal function of mismatch 
repair (MMR) system is the maintenance of genome integrity by 
correcting base pair mismatches and IDLs that escape the proof-
reading activity of DNA polymerases, and mismatches that occur 
after HR events (Modrich and Lahue, 1996; Jiricny, 1998; Schofield 
and Hsieh, 2003; Jun et al., 2006). MMR also responds to certain 
forms of DNA damage caused by alkylating agents, cisplatin, ROS, 
and UV radiation where it targets base pair mismatches following 
base mis-incorporation opposite DNA lesion (Earley and Crouse, 
1998; Colussi et al., 2002; Mazurek et al., 2002; Stojic et al., 2004). 
In this role of DNA damage surveillance, MMR proteins activate 
cell cycle checkpoints and apoptosis through interaction with sig-
naling kinases such as ATM (or ataxia-telangiectasia mutated), 
ATR (or ataxia telangiectasia and Rad3 related), and checkpoint 
kinases CHK1, and CHK2 (Wang and Qin, 2003; Stojic et al., 2004; 
Yoshioka et al., 2006; Liu et al., 2010). Individuals with defective 
MMR exhibit mutator phenotype (or increase in the mutation rate); 
microsatellite (or repetitive DNA sequence motifs) instability at 
mono-, di-, and trinucleotide repeats resulting in length changes 
of these motifs; and predisposition to hereditary Lynch cancer syn-
drome, also known as hereditary non-polyposis colorectal cancer 
(HNPCC), at an early age (Ionov et al., 1993; Shibata et al., 1994; 
Kolodner, 1995; Lynch and Lynch, 2000).
The principal steps of prokaryotic and eukaryotic MMR are 
damage recognition and binding to the mismatch, stabilizing of 
the binding by the addition of other proteins, cutting the DNA at 
a distance from the mismatch, excision past the mismatch, resyn-
thesis, and ligation (Fig. 9-3B). Organisms differ in their strategies 
to discriminate the parental template strand from the newly synthe-
sized daughter strand. These mechanisms are understood in Gram-
negative bacteria such as E. coli that utilize the DNA methylation 
at the N6 position of adenine in 5′-GATC-3′ sequences to direct the 
MMR-dependent strand excision to the newly synthesized, unmeth-
ylated DNA strand (Putnam, 2016). In higher eukaryotes the strand 
discrimination mechanisms are not completely understood; how-
ever, MMR is associated with eukaryotic replication machinery, 
suggesting that this interaction facilitates strand discrimination 
(Modrich, 1997; Mastrocola and Heinen, 2010; Pluciennik et al., 
2010). Data implicate PCNA (the “clamp”) together with replica-
tion factor C (RFC) complex (“clamp loader”) in early steps of 
eukaryotic MMR, as well as in gap repair synthesis. Following mis-
match recognition, the mutS homolog (MSH) heterodimers convert 
to ATP-dependent sliding clamps and recruit mutL homolog (MLH) 
heterodimers that together coordinate daughter strand excision. 
MutLα complex is a latent endonuclease activated by MutSα, RFC, 
and PCNA in a mismatch- and ATP-dependent manner (Kadyrov 
et al., 2006). DNA daughter segment flanked by two-strand breaks 
is then removed by exonuclease 1 and finally replaced by the pro-
cessive DNA synthesis machinery (Kadyrov et al., 2006).
Direct Repair (DR)  The error-free direct repair (DR) mecha-
nism is defined as the elimination of DNA and RNA damage using 
chemical reversion that does not require a nucleotide template, 
breaking of the phosphodiester backbone, or DNA synthesis (Nay 
and O’Connor, 2013). There are two distinct DR mechanisms in 
the majority of organisms: photoreactivation of UV-induced pyrim-
idine dimers by photolyases, and the removal of the alkyl groups 
from DNA and RNA by methyltransferases and dioxygenases.
Photolyases   have been identified in various organisms, but 
were lost early in the mammalian evolution and are not present 
in humans (Gerkema et al., 2013). DNA photolyases repair the 
UV-A- and UV-B-induced major pyrimidine dimers in DNA and 
RNA (Kim and Sancar, 1993). Photolyases bind to DNA contain-
ing a pyrimidine dimer in a light-independent reaction and absorb 
a photon to initiate the splitting of the dimer into two pyrimidines 
(Rupert et al., 1958; Rupert, 1961; Sancar, 1994).
Methylguanine DNA Methyltransferase (MGMT)  O6-Alkyl-
guanines are major cytotoxic and pro-mutagenic adducts produced 
by a variety of endogenous and exogenous alkylating chemicals 
(Loechler et al., 1984; Goldmacher et al., 1986; Beranek, 1990). If 
the cells enter S phase prior to O6-methylguanine (O6-MeG) repair, 
processive DNA polymerases commonly mispair O6-MeG with 
dT resulting in O6-MeG:T mismatch and fixation of a G:C-to-A:T 
transition mutation in the second round of replication (Fig. 9-4) 
(Ito et al., 1994). The ubiquitous methylguanine DNA methyltrans-
ferases (MGMT) remove alkyl from O6 position of dG. The human 
MGMT protein is constitutively expressed, housekeeping gene that 
is expressed in all tissues (Pieper, 1997). In all organisms, the alkyl 
group (such as methyl, ethyl, propyl, butyl, benzyl, and 2-chloro-
ethyl) is transferred via a one-step reaction to the repair protein by 
covalent binding to a highly conserved, active site cysteine directly 
restoring the DNA base and inactivating the protein (Tano et al., 
1990; Grombacher et al., 1996; Margison et al., 2003; Parkinson 
et al., 2008). This is referred to as a “suicide mechanism” where 
the protein is inactivated and targeted for elimination by protea-
some (Srivenugopal et al., 1996). A clear link between the levels 
of MGMT repair protein and the existence of thresholds for HPRT 
gene mutation induction was demonstrated in human-derived

510
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
AHH-1 cells (Thomas et al., 2013). When levels of MGMT are not 
adequate or depleted, the MMR pathway serves as a backup repair 
pathway by eliminating cells with O6-MeG bases and preventing 
mutation (Fig. 9-4) (Duckett et al., 1996; Kaina et al., 1997; Cejka 
et al., 2004). O6-alkylguanine adducts can also be repaired by NER 
pathway (Taira et al., 2013). Biochemical studies have revealed that 
O6-MeG can be bypassed by TLS polymerases Pol η, Pol κ, and 
Pol ξ (Ito et al., 1994).
Alkbh Fe(II)/α-Ketoglutarate-Dependent Dioxygenases  Direct 
repair of N3-cytosine and N1-adenine alkyl adducts in DNA and 
RNA has been associated with the action of alkylation protein B 
(AlkB) family of dioxygenases (Kataoka et al., 1983; Delaney 
et al., 2005; Frick et al., 2007). The AlkB enzyme was first dis-
covered in E. coli and a number of its homologues (ALKBH) have 
been identified in humans (Aravind and Koonin, 2001; Kurowski 
et al., 2003). DNA adducts that are repaired by these enzymes inter-
fere with base-pairing, block DNA replication and transcription, 
and trigger cell cycle checkpoints and apoptosis (Kataoka et al., 
1983; Kurowski et al., 2003; Westbye et al., 2008). Dealkylation 
reaction catalyzed by AlkB and ALKBH occurs via transformation 
of α-ketoglutarate into succinate and restoration of the undam-
aged base (Trewick et al., 2002; Lee et al., 2005); removal of 
methyl adducts releases formaldehyde, removal of ethyl adducts 
releases acetaldehyde, whereas removal of etheno adducts releases 
glyoxal (Trewick et al., 2002; Delaney and Essigmann, 2004). 
E. coli AlkB mutants and Alkbh2- and Alkbh3-deficient mouse 
embryonic fibroblasts exhibit increased sensitivity to SN2-type 
alkylating chemicals and increased mutant frequency (Delaney and 
Essigmann, 2004; Ringvoll et al., 2006; Nieminuszczy et al., 2009).
Translesion Synthesis (TLS)  Translesion synthesis (TLS) is 
carried out by 11 highly conserved and specialized DNA polymer-
ases that are lesion- and species-specific (Prakash et al., 2005). 
TLS involves a temporary switch from the replicative, high-­fidelity 
polymerases to specialized, error-prone polymerases that help the 
cells bypass and tolerate the unrepaired damage (Moldovan et 
al., 2007). Common characteristics of TLS polymerases are large 
active sites that accommodate the damage, fewer contacts with 
DNA, low processivity, and lack of 3′–5′ exonuclease activity. 
For example, yeast and human homologues of TLS Pol η repli-
cate past TT dimers in an error-free manner; however, these incor-
rectly insert dG across from thymidine in 6-4 pyrimidine dimer 
photoproducts causing T-to-C mutations (Masutani et al., 2000; 
Johnson et al., 2001). XPV (xeroderma pigmentosum variant) 
patients have demonstrated congenital lack of this Pol η that leads 
to UV-hypersensitivity and predisposition to cutaneous malignan-
cies (Tung et al., 1996; Masutani et al., 1999; Kannouche et al., 
2004). Two TLS models have been proposed: the polymerase 
switch model to accommodate lesion bypass, and single-strand 
gap-filling model that typically falls outside of S phase of cell cycle 
(Chatterjee and Walker, 2017). After nucleotides are incorporated 
across from the damaged bases, the aberrant primer termini are 
typically extended by Pol ζ.

Figure 9-4.  Interactions of DNA repair and damage response pathways in the processing and repair of O6-MeG adducts in DNA. MGMT repair protein directly 
repairs O6-MeG adducts. If unrepaired, O6-MeG preferably mispairs with T during DNA replication leading to G:C-to-A:T transitions. Alternatively, O6-MeG 
lesion induces apoptosis via the mismatch repair-dependent pathway. O6-MeG/mismatch repair-dependent DNA damage response includes multi-DNA damage 
response pathway, multi-time scale signaling network activation led by early ATM, H2AX, ATR-CHK1 kinases, and p53 phosphorylation, then followed by 
late phosphorylation of ATM-CHK2 and JNK kinase, as well as dramatic increases in p53 levels and p53 transcriptional target. Sister chromatid exchanges and 
chromosomal aberrations are induced by O6-MeG lesions via the mismatch repair-dependent pathway in the second cell cycle. The gaps and nicks present dur-
ing this phase can form double-strand breaks that are handled by homologous recombination. With loss of mismatch repair activity, cells become “methylation-

Genetic Toxicology

DNA Damage Response (DDR)  DNA damage response (DDR) 
networks effectively remove thousands of background DNA 
load and maintain a very low level of spontaneous mutagenesis 
(Chatterjee and Siede, 2013). DNA repair enzymes and signaling 
networks are regulated by DDR kinases including ATM (ataxia-
telangiectasia mutated), ATR (ATM- and Rad3-Related), and 
DNA-PKcs (DNA-dependent protein kinases) at the sites of DNA 
damage via posttranslational protein modification cascades. These 
kinases sense the damage together with their mediator proteins, 
then pass the damage signal onto the transducer checkpoint kinases 
CHK1 and CHK2 (Bartek et al., 2001; Chen and Sanchez, 2004; 
Tanaka, 2010), followed by direct or indirect mediators of cell cycle 
progression such as p53, cell division cycle 25 CDC25A, B, or C 
phosphatases (Falck et al., 2001; Zhao et al., 2002; Sørensen et al., 
2003). Activation of the p53 checkpoint provides the cell with a 
more prolonged G1 cell cycle arrest (Bartek and Lukas, 2001). The 
p53 protein is phosphorylated directly by ATM and ATR as well 
as by CHK1 and CHK2 to stabilize and increase the level of cel-
lular p53 (Shiloh, 2003). As most mutations in DNA occur when a 
cell with unrepaired damage enters S phase of the cell cycle, DDR 
checkpoints arrest the progression of cell cycle to allow additional 
time for excision repair of the damage, or for DNA damage avoid-
ance, or for TLS. Should the checkpoints fail to prevent polymerase 
stalling, or if the damage is too extensive, cells can undergo apopto-
sis to eliminate heavily damaged or dysregulated cells.
As cells continually respond to endogenous DNA damage, the 
adaptive and homeostatic responses have been shown to also be 
utilized following exposures to DNA-reactive agents at all exposure 
doses (Noonan et al., 2012; Tentner et al., 2012; Li et al., 2014; 
Zhang et al., 2015). The fast-acting posttranslational networks, 
involving signaling ATM, ATR, p38, CHK2, and p53 cascades, are 
the main contributors to the avoidance of genotoxic outcomes at 
endogenous and at low exogenous DNA damage load of various 
classes (Zhang et al., 2014, 2015). However, the transcriptional 
up-regulation of genes occurred at higher concentrations of bilin-
ear dose–response curves with clear increases in micronuclei 
formation (Seidel et al., 2004; Zhang et al., 2014, 2015). ATM 
signaling kinase and p53 tumor suppressor are key players in 
DDR, but cells can utilize parallel DDR signaling pathways that 
are independent of ATM and p53 cascades, underscoring the com-
plexity and interconnectedness of biological responses to DNA 
damage (Hickman and Samson, 1999; Debiak et al., 2004; Noonan 
et al., 2012).
Taken together, the probability that DNA damage can be con-
verted into a genetic alteration is influenced by the dose–response 
relationships of various lesion classes, activated pathways, the com-
plex biological interactions between these pathways, the rate of the 
damage repair, and the fidelity and completeness of the repair. The 
mechanisms of induction of gene mutations and chromosome alter-
ations discussed in the following sections build upon the assessment 
of the probability and the mechanistic knowledge of repair versus 
mis-repair versus non-repair of the different DNA repair and DDR 
mechanisms.
MECHANISMS OF INDUCTION OF GENETIC 
ALTERATIONS
Formation of Gene Mutations
Somatic Cells  Mutations result in changes to the structure of a 
gene (Lodish et al., 2000). Gene mutations are considered to be 
small DNA sequence changes confined to a single gene such as 
point mutations, which involve alterations in a single base pair 
that affect the function of only one gene, and small deletions/addi-
tions in any DNA region. Larger genomic changes are considered 
below, under the section “Formation of Chromosomal Alterations.” 
Changes in a single base pair may produce one of three types of 
mutation: missense mutation, which results in a protein with one 
amino acid substituted for another; nonsense mutation, in which 
a stop codon replaces an amino acid codon, leading to premature 
termination of translation; and frameshift mutation, which causes a 
change in the reading frame by an addition or deletion of one or a 
few base pairs (not in multiples of three) in protein-coding regions, 
leading to pre-mature termination of the protein (Ripley; 1991; 
Lodish et al., 2000). Base substitutions can be further subdivided 
as transitions where the change is purine for purine or pyrimidine 
for pyrimidine, and transversions where the change is purine for 
pyrimidine and vice versa. Historically, nonsense mutations where 
the stop codon in the transcribed RNA is UAA are termed as ochre 
mutations, those with UAG codon are called amber mutations, and 
those with UGA are referred to as opal mutations. For the discus-
sion of the mechanism of induction of gene mutations and chromo-
somal alterations, it is necessary to distinguish substances by their 
general mode of action (MOA). Chemicals that can produce genetic 
alterations with similar effectiveness in all stages of the cell cycle 
are called radiomimetic because they act like radiation; the radio-
mimetic group includes bleomycin, streptonigrin, neocarzinostatin, 
and 8-ethoxycaffeine. Mutations caused by such chemicals tend to 
be deletions caused by DNA strand breaks ranging in size from a 
few bases to multilocus events (Thacker, 1992). As discussed pre-
viously, DNA strand breaks are rapidly repaired by multiple repair 
processes before DNA replication takes place. Therefore, mutations 
induced by ionizing radiation tend to be the result of errors of DNA 
repair (Preston, 1992). In contrast, chemicals that produce genetic 
alterations far more effectively in the S phase of the cell cycle are 
described as nonradiomimetic. The great majority of chemicals are 
nonradiomimetic.
Gene mutations can arise in the absence of specific exog-
enous exposures to radiation and chemicals. The great majority 
of spontaneous mutations arise from polymerase slippage, DNA 
biosynthetic errors, and replication errors of an altered template; 
spontaneous altered template can be a result of oxidative and alkyl-
ated damage, or the deamination of bases especially cytosine in 
DNA/5-methylcytosine at CpG sites resulting in uracil or thymine, 
respectively, eventually leading to G:C-to-A:T transitions after two 
rounds of DNA replication (Shinde et al., 2003; Jinks-Robertson 
and Bhagwat, 2014).
Gene mutations induced by a majority of chemicals and non-
ionizing radiations are base substitutions, frameshifts, and small 
deletions produced by errors of DNA replication on a damaged 
template. The relative mutation frequency of an adduct will be the 
outcome of kinetics between its repair and DNA replication, that 
is, in general terms, the more repair that takes place prior to rep-
lication, the lower the mutation frequency for a given amount of 
induced DNA damage. Furthermore, the nature of an unrepaired 
adduct dictates its miscoding properties expressed by DNA poly-
merases during the S phase (Jarabek et al., 2009). Not all DNA 
adducts are miscoding; small alkyl adducts frequently found at the 
N7 position of guanine are not mutagenic and do not block DNA 
replication progress (Beranek, 1990; Boysen et al., 2009; Swenberg 
et al., 2011; Philippin et al., 2014). Activation of S phase at a G1/S 
cell cycle checkpoints through signaling cascades involving kinases 
such as ATM, ATR, CHEK1, CHK2, and p53 is an important modu-
lator of these kinetics, as more repair can take place prior to the 
cell starting/re-starting DNA replication (Mercer, 1998; Giglia-
Mari et al., 2011). Finally, error-prone translesion synthesis past

512
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
pro-mutagenic lesions can lead to mutations (Fix et al., 2008; 
Sale, 2013).
Germ Cells  Mutations in germ cells are changes to the DNA 
sequence of sperm or egg that can be inherited by the offspring 
(Yauk et al., 2015). The mechanism of production of gene muta-
tions in germ cells is basically the same as in somatic cells. 
It was estimated that a child inherits an average of one de novo 
mutation per 100×106 parental nucleotides (Roach et al., 2010; 
Conrad et  al., 2011). An important consideration for assessing 
gene mutations induced by chemicals in germ cells is the relation-
ship between exposure and the timing of DNA replication. In the 
mammalian spermatogonial stem cell, only a small fraction of the 
8-day or longer cell cycle is being occupied by the S phase; thus, 
the probability of DNA repair taking place prior to DNA replica-
tion is high. However, for considerations of the genetic risk, the 
spermatogonial stem cell is present throughout the reproductive 
lifetime of an individual; each time it divides, it produces a dif-
ferentiating spermatogonium and a stem cell that can accumulate 
genetic damage. Differentiating spermatogonia undergo a series of 
mitotic divisions before entering meiosis, followed by a series of 
morphological transformation to produce spermatozoa (Clermont, 
1972); the late spermatid/spermatozoa stages become DNA repair 
deficient (Marchetti and Wyrobek, 2005; Olsen et al., 2005). 
Postmeiotic germ cells are particularly sensitive to mutation induc-
tion by nonradiomimetic chemicals, especially following acute 
exposures (Russell, 2004). The fairly short duration of this stage 
(approximately 21 days in the mouse) means that their contribu-
tion to genetic risk following chronic exposures is quite small. The 
DNA damage incurred during late spermatid/spermatozoa stages 
can be repaired post-fertilization using the egg’s repair proteins 
(Marchetti et al., 2007). In contrast, oocyte is resistant to the induc-
tion of gene mutations by nonradiomimetic chemicals for which 
DNA repair is the mode of formation of mutations, and DNA repair 
occurs in oocytes as there is no further S phase until the zygote 
(Brewen and Preston, 1982).
The mechanistic aspects of the production of heritable gene 
mutations in male premeiotic germ cells has recently been consid-
ered for monofunctional alkylating chemicals and presented as an 
adverse outcome pathway (AOP) (Yauk et al., 2015). In general, 
AOPs describe a simplified series of key events (KEs) along the 
biological organization of organisms that lead to an adverse health 
or population effects (Villeneuve et al., 2014a, 2014b). An AOP 
links a molecular initiating event (MIE) to an adverse outcome 
(AO) through intermediate KEs. The tailored Bradford-Hill consid-
erations for key event relationships (KERs) and evidence evaluation 
of the essentiality of KEs to AO are systematically assessed under 
this framework. The AOP for alkylation of DNA in male premeiotic 
germ cells leading to heritable mutations is presented in Fig. 9-5. 
The MIE for this AOP has been designated as the alkylation of 
DNA in male premeiotic germ cells. This MIE is followed by the 
KE1: insufficient or incorrect DNA repair in male premeiotic germ 
cells, and KE2: increased mutations in male premeiotic germ cells. 
Mutations that do not impair spermatogenesis persist and eventually 
are present in mature sperm can be measured with in vivo trans-
genic rodent mutation (OECD TG 488, 2013) assay or mutations 
in simple tandem repeat DNA sequences (Yauk, 2004). Eventually, 
these series of KEs lead to an adverse outcome (AO) of increased 
heritable mutations in the offspring. Inherited mutations in the off-
spring can lead to embryonic or fetal death, or be transmitted to the 
offspring and cause phenotypic outcomes; thus, heritable mutation 
is an important regulatory endpoint used by the agencies around 
the world for risk assessment and regulatory purposes (Vinken, 
2013; Yauk et al., 2015). The overall AOP is generally accepted 
as dogma with strong essentiality of its KEs and KERs as well as 
strong biological plausibility without notable inconsistencies and 
uncertainties.
Formation of Chromosomal Alterations
Somatic Cells 
Structural Chromosome Aberrations  Large-scale changes 
in chromosome structure can affect the functioning of numer-
ous genes, resulting in major phenotypic consequences (Lodish 
et al., 2000). These chromosomal mutations, or abnormalities, can 
involve deletion or insertion of several contiguous genes, inversion 
of genes on a chromosome, or the exchange of large segments of 
DNA between nonhomologous chromosomes. Chromosome aber-
rations induced by ionizing radiations are generally formed by 
errors of DNA repair, whereas those produced by nonradiomimetic 
chemicals are generally formed by errors of DNA replication on a 
damaged DNA template.

Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
The DNA repair errors that lead to the formation of chromo-
some aberrations following ionizing radiation (and radiomimetic 
chemical) exposure arise from misligation of double-strand breaks 
or interaction of coincidentally repairing regions during excision 
repair of damaged bases. The overall kinetics and fidelity of DNA 
repair influence the sensitivity of cells to the induction of chromo-
somal aberrations produced by misrepair. The broad outcomes of 
misrepair are that incorrect rejoining of chromosomal pieces dur-
ing repair leads to chromosomal exchanges within (e.g., inversions 
and interstitial deletions) and between (e.g., dicentrics and recipro-
cal translocations) chromosomes. Failure to rejoin double-strand 
breaks or to complete the repair of other types of DNA damage 
leads to terminal deletions.
Acentric fragments arise from interstitial deletions, terminal 
deletions, and the formation of dicentric chromosomes and rings. The 
failure to incorporate an acentric fragment into a daughter nucleus at 
anaphase/telophase, or the failure of a whole chromosome to segre-
gate at anaphase to the cellular poles, can result in the formation of a 
membrane-bound micronucleus that resides in the cytoplasm.
Errors of DNA replication on a damaged template can lead to 
a variety of chromosomal alterations. The majority of these involve 
deletion or exchange of individual chromatids (chromatid-type aber-
rations). Thus, nonradiomimetic chemicals induce only chromatid-
type aberrations, whereas radiation and radiomimetic chemicals 
induce chromatid-type aberrations in the S and G2 phases of the cell 
cycle, but chromosome-type aberrations affecting both chromatids in 
G1. The reason for this latter observation is that the G1 (or G0) chro-
mosome behaves as a single DNA molecule and aberrations formed 
in it will be replicated in the S phase and will involve both chro-
matids. This distinction is important for considerations of outcome 
of the aberrations and the probability of an effect on cells because 
for chromatid-type aberrations, one chromatid remains intact and 
genetically unaltered, in contrast to chromosome-type aberrations in 
which both chromatids are damaged (Preston et al., 1995).
Numerical Chromosome Changes  Numerical changes (e.g., 
monosomies, trisomies, and ploidy changes) can arise from errors 
in chromosomal segregation. The complexity of the control and the 
mechanics of the mitotic process means that alteration of various cel-
lular components can result in failure to segregate the sister chroma-
tids to separate daughter cells or in failure to segregate a chromosome 
to either pole (Bickel and Orr-Weaver, 1996; Preston, 1996; Hunt, 
2006). The mechanisms underlying chromosomal loss are pertinent 
to those involved in the formation of micronuclei. Aneuploidy is a 
known cause of human disease and represents a major cause of infer-
tility, pregnancy failure, and serious genetic disorders in the offspring.
A limited set of chemicals has been demonstrated to cause 
aneuploidy through interaction with components of the structures 
that facilitate chromosome movement (Preston, 1996; Aardema 
et al., 1998). These include benomyl, griseofulvin, nocodazole, 
colchicine, colecemid, vinblastine, and paclitaxel. These chemicals 
affect tubulin polymerization or spindle microtubule stability. A 
group of small molecule drugs that are targeted to inhibit Aurora 
kinases, a family of serine/threonine kinases important for proper 
attachment of microtubules at spindle assembly during cell divi-
sion, have been shown to induce aneuploidy (Gollapudi et al., 2014; 
Bavetsias and Linardopoulos, 2015). To date, other mechanisms of 
aneuploidy induction by chemicals have not been firmly identified.
Germ Cells  The formation of chromosomal alterations in germ 
cells is basically the same as that for somatic cells, namely, via 
misrepair for ionizing radiations and radiomimetic chemicals for 
treatments in G1 and G2, and by errors of replication for all radia-
tions and chemicals for DNA damage present during the S phase.
The types of aberrations formed in germ cells are the same as 
those formed in somatic cells (e.g., deletions, inversions, transloca-
tions), although their appearance in diplotene/diakinesis of meiosis 
I, where analysis is frequently conducted, is rather different because 
of the homologous chromosome pairing that takes place in meiotic 
cells (Léonard, 1973). The specific segregation of chromosomes 
during meiosis influences the probability of recovery of an aber-
ration, particularly a reciprocal translocation, in the offspring of a 
treated parent (Preston et al., 1995).
An adverse outcome pathway (AOP) for chemical binding to 
tubulin in oocytes leading to aneuploid offspring has been developed 
and published (Marchetti et al., 2016). Colchicine, a prototypical 
chemical that binds to tubulin and causes microtubule depolymer-
ization has been used to develop the AOP. In this AOP specific to 
female germ cells exposed during the periovulation period, chemi-
cals that bind to tubulin (MIE) cause microtubule depolymerization 
(KE1) resulting in spindle disorganization (KE2) followed by altered 
chromosome alignment and segregation (KE3) and the generation 
of aneuploid oocytes (KE4), ultimately leading to an increased inci-
dence of aneuploidy syndromes in the offspring (AO). The available 
evidence suggests that the MIE and KEs are conserved across species 
and would be relevant for human oocytes. The development of AOPs 
related to mutagenicity in germ cells is expected to aid the identifica-
tion of potential hazards to germ cell genomic integrity and support 
regulatory efforts to protect population health (Marchetti et al., 2016).
ASSAYS FOR DETECTING GENETIC 
ALTERATIONS
Introduction to Assay Design
Genetic toxicology assays are used to identify germ cell mutagens, 
somatic cell mutagens, and potential carcinogens. These assays can 
detect diverse kinds of genetic alterations (e.g., gene mutations, 
chromosome aberrations, and aneuploidy) that are relevant to the 
production of adverse human health outcomes. Over the last three 
decades, hundreds of chemicals and complex mixtures have been 
evaluated for genotoxic effects. Genetic toxicology assays serve 
two interrelated but distinct purposes in the toxicologic evaluation 
of chemicals: (1) identifying mutagens for purposes of hazard iden-
tification and (2) characterizing dose–response relationships and 
mutagenic mechanisms, both of which contribute to an understand-
ing of genetic and carcinogenic risks.
A common experience when surveying the genetic toxicology 
literature is encountering a bewildering array of assays in viruses, 
bacteria, fungi, cultured mammalian cells, plants, insects, and mam-
mals. More than 200 assays for mutagens have been proposed, and 
useful information has been obtained from many of them. Although 
most genetic toxicology testing and evaluation relies on relatively 
few assays, data from relatively obscure assays can sometimes con-
tribute to a judgment about the genetic activity of a compound.
Table 9-1 lists key assays that have a prominent place in 
genetic toxicology. Table 9-2 is a more comprehensive list that 
provides literature citations to many of the assays that one might 
encounter in the genetic toxicology literature. Even this extensive 
table is not exhaustive, in that it emphasizes methods in applied 
genetic toxicology and not those assays whose use has been largely 
restricted to studies of mutational mechanisms. The commonly used 
assays rely on phenotypic effects as indicators of gene mutations 
or small deletions and on cytological methods for observing gross 
chromosomal damage. Detailed information on assay design, test-
ing data, controls, sample sizes, and other factors in effective testing 
is found in the references cited.

514
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
Some assays for gene mutations detect forward mutations 
whereas others detect reversion. Forward mutations, such as those 
detected in the thymidine kinase (tk) gene in the widely used assay in 
mouse lymphoma cells (Clements, 2000; Wang et al., 2009; Schisler 
et al., 2013), are genetic alterations in a wild type gene that are 
detected by a change in phenotype caused by the alteration or loss of 
gene function. In contrast, reversion mutations are those that restore 
gene function in a mutant, such as the histidine revertants detected in 
the Ames assay in Salmonella (Ames et al., 1975; Mortelmans and 
Zeiger, 2000; Claxton et al., 2010). Thus, a back mutation or rever-
sion that restores gene function in a mutant brings about a return 
to the wild type phenotype. In principle, forward-mutation assays 
should respond to a broad spectrum of mutagens because any muta-
tion that interferes with gene expression should confer the detect-
able phenotype. A reversion assay might be expected to have a more 
restricted mutational response because only mutations that correct or 
compensate for the specific mutation in a particular mutant will be 
detected. In fact, some reversion assays respond to a broader spec-
trum of mutational changes than one might expect because mutations 
at a site other than that of the original mutation, either within the test 
gene or in a different gene (i.e., a suppressor mutation), can some-
times confer the selected phenotype. Both forward mutation assays 
and reversion assays are used extensively in genetic toxicology.
The simplest gene mutation assays rely on selection techniques 
to detect mutations. A selection technique is a means of imposing 
experimental conditions under which only cells or organisms that 
have undergone mutation can grow. For example, only cells that have 
a mutation in the tk gene can grow in medium containing the inhibitory 
chemical trifluorothymidine (Seifried et al., 2006; Wang et al., 2009; 
Schisler et al., 2013). Selection techniques greatly facilitate the identi-
fication of rare cells that have experienced mutation among the many 
cells that have not. Forward mutations (Clements, 2000; Vlasakova 
et al., 2005) and reversions (Josephy, 2000; Mortelmans and Riccio, 
2000; Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000; Kamber et al., 2009) can both be 
detected by selection techniques in microorganisms and cultured mam-
malian cells. Due to their speed, low cost, and ease of detecting events 
that occur at low frequency (i.e., mutation), assays in microorganisms 
and cell cultures have figured prominently in genetic toxicology.
Studying mutagenesis in intact animals requires assays of 
more complex design than the simple selection methods used in

microorganisms and cultured cells. Genetic toxicology assays there-
fore range from inexpensive short-term tests (Zeiger, 2010) that can 
be performed in a few days to complicated assays for mutations in 
mammalian germ cells (Favor, 1999; Russell, 2004; Singer et al., 
2006). Even in multicellular organisms, there has been an empha-
sis on designing assays that detect mutations with great efficiency 
(Casciano et al., 1999; Vogel et al., 1999; Lambert et al., 2005; 
Dobrovolsky et al., 2010). Nevertheless, there remains a gradation 
in which an increase in relevance for human risk entails more elabo-
rate and costly tests (Table 9-2). The most expensive mammalian 
tests are typically reserved for chemicals of special importance in 
basic research or risk assessment, whereas the simpler assays can 
be applied more broadly.
Cytogenetic assays differ in design from typical gene mutation 
assays because of their reliance on cytological rather than genetic 
methods. The goal in cytogenetic methods is the unequivocal 
visual recognition of cells that have experienced genetic damage. 
The alterations measured include chromosome aberrations (Preston 
et al., 1981; Ishidate et al., 1988; Corvi et al., 2008), micronuclei 
(Hayashi et al., 2007; Corvi et al., 2008; Fenech et al., 2011a, 
2011b), sister chromatid exchanges (SCEs; Tucker et al., 1993a; 
Wilson and Thompson, 2007), and changes in chromosome num-
ber (Aardema et al., 1998; Paccierotti and Sgura, 2008). The latter 
include ploidy changes (e.g., polyploid cells) and aneuploidy, in 
which one or a few chromosomes have been gained (i.e., hyper-
ploidy) or lost (i.e., hypoploidy) relative to the normal chromosome 
number. Aneuploidy is of great interest because of its implications 
for human health, but assays for aneuploidy are not yet as refined 
or systematically applied as those for other classes of chromosomal 
alterations. The biological and toxicological significance of SCEs is 
largely unknown and as such tests for SCE induction are no longer 
included as part of genotoxicity assessment.
In all mutagenicity testing, one must be aware of possible 
sources of error. Factors to consider in the application of muta-
genicity assays are the choice of suitable organisms and growth 
conditions, appropriate monitoring of genotypes and phenotypes,

unit III
effective experimental design and treatment conditions, inclusion 
of proper positive and negative controls, and sound methods of data 
analysis. The articles cited in Table 9-2 discuss these aspects of 
genetic toxicology testing. In addition, the publication by Lorge 
et al. (2016) provides guidance on good cell culture practices in 
the conduct of genetic toxicology assays using mammalian cell 
cultures.
Many substances that are not themselves mutagenic or carci-
nogenic can be activated into mutagens and carcinogens by metabo-
lism (Guengerich, 2001; Malling, 2004). Such chemicals are called 
promutagens and procarcinogens. Since microorganisms and mam-
malian cell cultures lack many of the metabolic capabilities of intact 
mammals, provision must be made for metabolic activation in order 
to detect promutagens in many genetic assays. Incorporating an 
in vitro metabolic activation system derived from a mammalian 
tissue homogenate is the most common means of adding meta-
bolic activation to microbial or cell culture assays (Malling and 
Frantz, 1973; Ames et al., 1975; Clements, 2000; Malling, 2004; 
Cox et al., 2016). For example, the promutagens dimethylnitrosa-
mine and benzo[a]pyrene are not themselves mutagenic in bacte-
ria, but they are mutagenic in bacterial assays if the bacteria are 
treated with the promutagen in the presence of a homogenate from 
mammalian liver.
The most widely used metabolic activation system in micro-
bial and cell culture assays is a postmitochondrial supernatant from 
a rat liver homogenate, along with appropriate buffers and cofactors 
(Maron and Ames, 1983; Kirkland et al., 1990). The standard liver 
metabolic activation system is called an S9 mixture, designating 
a supernatant from centrifugation at 9000 g (Malling and Frantz, 
1973; Maron and Ames, 1983). The S9 subcellular fraction con-
tains microsomes and cytosol with microsomes containing enzymes 
necessary for Phase I metabolism (e.g., cytochrome P450s) and 
cytosol containing the enzymes involved in Phase II metabolism. 
Most of the short-term assays in Table 9-2 require exogenous meta-
bolic activation to detect promutagens. Exceptions are assays in 
intact mammals and a few simpler assays that have a high level of 
endogenous cytochrome P450 metabolism, such as the detection of 
UDS or DNA strand breakage in cultured hepatocytes (Madle et al., 
1994; Gealy et al., 2007).
Rat liver S9, usually prepared following induction with 
Aroclor-1254 (a mixture of polychlorinated biphenyls) or 
phenobarbital/β-naphthoflavone, provides a broad assemblage 
of metabolic reactions, but they are not necessarily the same as 
those of hepatic metabolism in an intact animal (Richardson et al., 
2016). Metabolic activation systems based on homogenates from 
mice, guinea pigs, hamsters, or monkeys and preparations from 
organs other than liver have found some use in mutagenicity test-
ing (Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000). In some cases, these systems 
detect mutagenicity more efficiently than rat liver S9. However, 
like a homogenate from rat liver, these systems may differ from the 
species or organs of their origin. Therefore, alternative metabolic 
activation systems tend to be more useful if chosen for mechanistic 
reasons rather than simply testing another species or organ. Such 
systems include the use of intact hepatocytes (Madle et al., 1994; 
Storer et al., 1996; Gealy et al., 2007) to preserve the cellular com-
partmentalization of reactions; an in vitro system that can carry out 
the reduction reactions needed to detect the activity of substances 
whose mutagenicity requires reductive metabolism, such as some 
azo compounds (Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000; Seifried et al., 
2006); and the use of mammalian cells or bacteria engineered to 
express foreign genes that encode enzymes of metabolic activation 
(Sawada and Kamataki, 1998; Crespi and Miller, 1999; Josephy, 
2000, 2002; Oda et al., 2009).
Besides metabolic activation, some chemicals are subject 
to photochemical activation. The genotoxicity of such chemi-
cals depends on the chemical being irradiated with ultraviolet or 
visible light. Many of the assays listed in Table 9-2, including 
gene-­mutation assays in bacteria and cultured mammalian cells, 
cytogenetic assays, and the comet assay, have been adapted so that 
they can measure photogenotoxic effects (Brendler-Schwaab et al., 
2004; Lynch et al., 2011).
Genes encoding enzymes of xenobiotic metabolism have 
been incorporated by recombinant DNA technology into micro-
organisms or cell cultures to expand their capacity for metabolic 
activation. The genes incorporated into bacteria may be derived 
from other bacteria or from humans (Josephy, 2002; Oda et al., 
2009). For example, bacterial genes that cause overexpression of 
N-acetyltransferase enhance the sensitivity of the bacteria to the 
mutagenicity of aromatic amines or nitroarenes (Josephy, 2002). 
The expression of human cytochrome P450 enzymes in Salmonella 
tester strains from the Ames assay (Josephy 2002; Yamazaki et al., 
2004), a Salmonella SOS-induction assay (Oda et al., 2009), or 
E. coli strains of the lacZ reversion assay (Josephy, 2000) permits 
the activation of promutagens like 2-aminoanthracene and 2-ami-
nofluorene without a S9 mixture. Mammalian cell lines have also 
been genetically engineered to express human Phase-I and Phase-II 
enzymes, including those catalyzing reactions of metabolic activa-
tion (Sawada and Kamataki, 1998; Chevereau et al., 2017). Many 
cell lines stably expressing a single form of P450 have been estab-
lished. Mutagenesis can be measured through such endpoints as 
HPRT mutations, cytogenetic alterations, and γH2AX, and the cells 
are well suited to analyzing the contribution of different enzymes to 
the activation of promutagens (Crespi and Miller, 1999; Chevereau 
et al., 2017).
Metabolic activation is so central to genetic toxicology that all 
mutagenicity testing programs must provide for it in the choice of 
assays and procedures. Despite their usefulness, in vitro metabolic 
activation systems, however well refined, cannot mimic mammalian 
metabolism perfectly. There are differences among tissues in reac-
tions that activate or inactivate foreign compounds, and organisms 
of the normal flora of the intestines can contribute to metabolism in 
intact mammals. Chemicals that induce enzyme systems or other-
wise alter the physiological state can also modify the metabolism 
of toxicants, and the balance between activation and detoxification 
reactions in vitro may differ from that found in vivo.
Structural Alerts and In Silico Assays
The first indication that a chemical is a mutagen often lies in 
chemical structure. Potential electrophilic sites in a molecule serve 
as an alert to possible mutagenicity and carcinogenicity because 
such sites confer reactivity with nucleophilic sites in DNA (Miller 
and Miller, 1981; Tennant and Ashby, 1991; Snyder et al., 2004; 
Votano et al., 2004; Snyder and Smith, 2005). In general, genotoxic 
carcinogens typically are either electrophiles or can be activated 
to electrophilic reactive intermediates (pro-electrophiles). These 
compounds form covalent adducts with most of the cellular mac-
romolecules such as DNA, RNA, or proteins at the nucleophilic 
centers with electron-rich heteroatoms S, O, or N (Plošnik et al., 
2016). The understanding of the mechanisms of action through 
which electrophiles react with biological nucleophiles is based 
on the classical chemical reactions (SN1, SN1, acylation, Schiff 
base formation, Michael addition, and SNAr) between electron-
rich and electron-deficient centers (Plošnik et al., 2016). Strong 
electrophiles (e.g., nitroso compounds, epoxides, α,β- unsatu-
rated aldehydes, N-sulphonyloxy-N-methyl-4- aminoazobenzene,

519
Genetic Toxicology
Chapter 9
N-sulphonyloxy-N-acetyl-2-aminofluorene, and N-hydroxy-2-
aminofluorene) are small, poorly polarized molecules, and their 
electron deficiency shows as positive electron charge. Soft electro­
philes (e.g., safrole, estragole, N-methylol-4-aminoazobenzene) are 
usually large, highly polarized molecules, and their electron defi-
ciency spreads all over the molecule (Plošnik et al., 2016).
Computer-based systems for predicting genotoxicity based 
on chemical structure properties are sometimes called in silico 
assays. These assays include computational and structural programs 
(Snyder and Smith, 2005; Snyder, 2009; Knudsen et al., 2011; 
Mahadevan et al., 2011) and the modeling of quantitative structure–
activity relationships or (Q)SARs (Votano et al., 2004; Snyder and 
Smith, 2005; Mahadevan et al., 2011). The electrophilic theory of 
genotoxic carcinogenicity has led to two main (Q)SAR approaches 
for modeling genotoxic chemicals: (a) to identify the electrophilic 
functional groups or substructures, that is, to develop SAR models 
based on structural alerts; and (b) to find molecular descriptors that 
can be quantitatively related to the activity of the chemicals, that is, 
to develop (Q)SARs (Serafimova et al., 2010).
Structural alerts in combination with critical interpretation are 
a valuable initial adjunct to mutagenicity testing within the inte-
grated approaches for testing and assessment of chemicals (Tennant 
and Ashby, 1991; Ashby and Paton, 1993). Although there is much 
skepticism that such approaches can replace biological testing, 
they hold promise of improving the efficiency of integrated testing 
strategies and reducing current levels of animal use (Guha, 2011; 
Dearfield et al., 2017; Wichard, 2017). The most commonly mod-
eled endpoint for genotoxicity has been the Ames mutagenicity 
assay. The accuracy of Ames mutagenicity prediction is typically 
around 70% to 75% (Serafimova et al., 2010). (Q)SAR predictions 
should be interpreted by an expert with knowledge of the endpoint, 
the strengths and limitations of the specific model applied, the 
applicability domain of the model, and the reliability of prediction 
for the chemical of interest (Serafimova et al., 2010). Moreover, 
computational approaches should not be based on stand-alone basis, 
but on a weight-of-evidence approach including information from 
all available sources such as read across and in vitro test methods 
(Serafimova et al., 2010).
DNA Damage and Repair Assays
Some assays measure DNA damage itself, rather than mutational 
consequences of DNA damage. These assays belong to the category 
of “indicator assays” and the effects measured in these assays may 
or may not lead to mutational consequence. Damage to DNA may 
be induced directly, through such indicators as chemical adducts 
or strand breaks in DNA, or indirectly, through the measure-
ment of biological repair processes. Adducts in DNA are detected 
by 
32P-postlabeling, high-performance liquid chromatography 
(HPLC), fluorescence-based methods, mass spectrometry, immu-
nological methods using antibodies against specific adducts, iso-
tope-labeled DNA binding, and electrochemical detection (Phillips 
et al., 2000; Farmer and Singh, 2008; Himmelstein et al., 2009). 
The 32P-postlabeling method is highly versatile, in that it is sensi-
tive and can be applied to diverse mutagens, but it may fall short of 
other methods for quantitative accuracy (Farmer and Singh, 2008). 
Through a combination of methods, many classes of adducts, 
including those of such environmentally widespread compounds 
as polynuclear aromatic hydrocarbons, can be detected. The mea-
surement of adducts after human chemical exposures has proven 
useful in human monitoring, molecular dosimetry, and risk assess-
ment (Phillips et al., 2000; Farmer and Singh, 2008; Himmelstein 
et al., 2009). Adducts in somatic cells are relevant to carcinogenesis 
(Himmelstein et al., 2009), whereas those in reproductive cells per-
mit molecular dosimetry for germ cell mutagenesis (Olsen et al., 
2010; Verhofsrad et al., 2011; Yauk et al., 2015).
DNA strand breakage can be measured by alkaline elution 
(Storer et al., 1996; Gealy et al., 2007) and electrophoretic meth-
ods. Single-cell gel electrophoresis, also called the comet assay, is 
a widely used, rapid method of measuring DNA damage (Fairbairn 
et al., 1995; Singh, 2000; Tice et al., 2000; Collins, 2004; Olive, 
2009; Speit et al., 2015; Glei et al., 2016; Collins et al., 2017). 
In this assay, cells are incorporated into agarose on slides, lysed 
so as to liberate their DNA, and subjected to electrophoresis typi-
cally under alkaline conditions (pH >13) to allow detection of 
DNA double-strand breaks, single-strand breaks, alkali-labile sites, 
DNA-DNA/DNA-protein cross-linking, and incomplete excision 
repair sites (Fairbairn et al., 1995; Singh, 2000; Tice et al., 2000; 
Pu et al., 2015). The DNA is stained with a fluorescent dye for 
observation and image analysis. As broken DNA fragments in the 
tail migrate more quickly than larger pieces of DNA of the head, 
a blur of fragments (a “comet”) is observed when the DNA is 
extensively damaged. The extent of DNA damage can be estimated 
from the intensity of the comet tail relative to the total intensity 
(head plus tail) that reflects the amount of DNA breakage (Collins, 
2004; OECD TG 489, 2016). Variations in the procedure permit 
the preferential detection of double-strand breaks under neutral 
conditions (Fairbairn et al., 1995; Olive, 2009) or inclusion of 
digestion of lesion-specific DNA repair enzymes in the procedure 
allows the detection of various DNA base alterations, such as oxida-
tive base damage. (Pu et al., 2015; Glei et al., 2016; Collins et al., 
2017). Comet assay has also been modified to detect DNA meth-
ylation status (Townsend et al., 2017). Finally, the high-throughput 
“CometChip” technique, based on the traditional comet assay, 
simultaneously follows DNA damage load over time in different 
cell lines in response to various genotoxicants (Weingeist et al., 
2013; Ge et al., 2014). This method quantifies kinetics, differen-
tial sensitivity, and repair capacity within different genetic back-
grounds at the level of an individual cell. Although the comet assay 
has proven to be a sensitive indicator of DNA damage with broad 
applicability, it is prone to yield false positive/uninterpretable find-
ings under conditions of physiological and/or mechanical stress. It 
has been used most commonly with human lymphocytes (Fairbairn 
et al., 1995; Singh, 2000; Collins, 2004) and other mammalian cells 
(Tice et al., 2000), but it can be adapted to diverse species, including 
plants, worms, mollusks, fish, and amphibians (Cotelle and Férard, 
1999; Lee and Steinert, 2003; Jha, 2004). This adaptability makes 
it well suited to use in environmental genetic toxicology. The appli-
cability of the comet assay and other DNA damage assays to rodent 
testes (Bentley et al., 1994; Speit et al., 2009) makes these methods 
helpful in interpreting risks to germ cells.
The occurrence of DNA repair can serve as an easily measured 
indicator of DNA damage. Repair assays have been developed in 
microorganisms, cultured mammalian cells, and intact mammals 
(Table 9-2). Greater toxicity of a chemical in DNA-repair-deficient 
strains than in their repair-proficient counterparts (e.g., rec+ and 
rec− in Bacillus subtilis) can serve as an indicator of DNA dam-
age in bacteria (Takigami et al., 2002). Bacterial repair assays find 
occasional application, but are used less commonly today than his-
torically. The measurement of unscheduled DNA synthesis (UDS), 
which is a measure of excision repair, is a mammalian DNA repair 
assay indicating that DNA has been damaged (Madle et al., 1994). 
UDS has been used with cultured hepatocytes with endogenous 
cytochrome P450 enzyme activities as well as tissues of intact ani-
mals, including hepatocytes (Madle et al., 1994), and germinal tis-
sue (Bentley et al., 1994; Sotomayor and Sega, 2000). The absence

520
Non-Organ-Directed toxicity
unit III
of UDS, however, does not provide evidence that DNA has not been 
damaged because some classes of damage are not readily excised. 
Based upon their analysis, Kirkland and Speit (2008) questioned the 
value of in vivo rodent liver UDS assay as follow-up to the positive 
findings of in vitro genotoxicity assays. Their analyses were based 
on the relative performance of three in vivo assays (liver comet, 
transgenic mutation, and bone marrow micronucleus) in predicting 
rodent carcinogens.
Besides specific DNA repair processes, the induction of 
general responses to genotoxic stress has been used as an indica-
tor of genetic damage. The induction of DNA repair networks, 
known as the “SOS response,” allows bacteria to survive sudden 
increases in DNA damage as detected by phage induction, colo-
rimetry, or green fluorescent protein (GFP) reporter induction after 
the activation of SOS gene promoters (Quillardet and Hofnung, 
1993; Yasunaga et al., 2004; Friedman et al., 2005; Michel, 2005; 
Oda et al., 2009). Another fluorescence-based screen, the growth 
arrest and DNA damage gene GADD45a-GFP assay, also called 
“Green Screen,” detects genotoxic stress in human lymphoblastoid 
TK6 cells (Hastwell et al., 2009; Hughes et al., 2012). The stress 
response is detected by the genetic GFP reporter construct following 
p53-dependent expression from GADD45a promoter of the trun-
cated GADD45a gene/GFP construct. The induction of fluorescence 
by mutagens, clastogens, and aneugens exhibits sensitivity toward 
genotoxicants comparable with other in vitro mammalian cell 
assays as well as a very high specificity (93%) (Birrell et al., 2009). 
The assay can be conducted with S9 metabolic activation and lends 
itself to automated detection by flow cytometry (Jagger et al., 2009).
Gene Mutations in Prokaryotes
The most common means of detecting mutations in microorganisms 
is selecting for reversion in strains that have a specific nutritional 
requirement differing from wild type members of the species; such 
strains are called auxotrophs. For example, the widely used assay 
developed by Bruce Ames and his colleagues is based on measur-
ing reversion in several histidine auxotrophs in Salmonella enterica 
serovar Typhimurium, commonly called S. typhimurium (Ames 
et al., 1975).
In the Ames assay one measures the frequency of histidine-
independent bacteria that arise in a histidine-requiring strain in the 
presence or absence of the chemical being tested. Auxotrophic bac-
teria are treated with the chemical of interest by one of several pro-
cedures (e.g., the standard plate-incorporation assay) and plated on 
medium that is deficient in histidine (Ames et al., 1975; Maron and 
Ames, 1983; Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000). The assay is conducted 
using genetically different strains so that reversion by base pair sub-
stitutions and frameshift mutations in several DNA sequence con-
texts can be detected and distinguished. Since Salmonella does not 
metabolize promutagens in the same way as mammalian tissues, 
the assay is generally performed in the presence and absence of a 
rat liver S9 metabolic activation system. The Ames assay is com-
monly referred to as the Salmonella–Escherichia coli/mammalian-
microsome reverse mutation assay although the activation system 
used is S9 (microsomes + cytosol) (OECD TG 471, 1997). Based 
on the current version of the OECD Ames test guideline, at least 
five strains of bacteria should be used that include four strains of 
S. typhimurium that revert at GC base pairs (TA1535; TA1537 or 
TA97a or TA97; TA98; and TA100) and the fifth strain that reverts at 
AT base pair (either one of E. coli WP2 uvrA, or E. coli WP2 uvrA 
(pKM101), or S. typhimurium TA102).
The principal strains of the Ames test and their characteristics 
are summarized in Table 9-3. In addition to the histidine alleles that 
provide the target for measuring mutagenesis, the Ames tester strains 
contain other genes and plasmids that enhance the assay. Part I of the 
table gives genotypes, and Part II explains the rationale for including 
specific genetic characteristics in the strains. Part III summarizes the 
principal DNA target in each strain and the mechanisms of rever-
sion. Taken together, the Ames strains detect a broad array of muta-
tions, and they complement one another. For example, E. coli and S. 
typhimurium oxidative damage-sensitive strains TA102 and TA104, 
all detect the A:T-to-G:C base pair substitutions that are not detected 
by hisG46 strains (Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000). TA102 also detects 
chemicals that cause DNA cross-links because it has an intact exci-
sion repair system whereas the other common tester strains do not.
The most common version of the Ames assay is the plate-
incorporation test (Ames et al., 1975; Maron and Ames, 1983; 
Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000). In this procedure, the bacterial tester 
strain, the test compound (or solvent control), and the S9 metabolic 
activation system (or buffer for samples without S9) are added to 
2 mL of molten agar containing biotin and a trace of histidine to 
allow a few cell divisions, mixed, and immediately poured onto the 
surface of a petri dish selective for histidine-independent revertants. 
For general testing it is recommended that at least three plates per 
concentration and five concentrations be used with and without S9, 
along with appropriate concurrent positive and negative controls 
(Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000). Variations on the standard plate-
incorporation assay confer advantages for some applications. These 
include a preincubation assay that facilitates the detection of unsta-
ble compounds and short-lived metabolites, a desiccator assay for 
testing volatile chemicals and gases, a microsuspension assay for 
working with small quantities of test materials, assays incorporating 
reductive metabolism rather than the conventional S9 system, and 
assays under hypoxic conditions (Mortelmans and Zeiger, 2000).
Although simplicity is a great merit of microbial assays, it can 
also be deceptive. Even assays that are simple in design and applica-
tion can be performed incorrectly. For example, in the Ames assay one 
may see very small colonies in the petri dishes at highly toxic concen-
trations (Maron and Ames, 1983; Kirkland et al., 1990; Mortelmans 
and Zeiger, 2000). Counting such colonies as revertants would be 
an error because they may actually be non-revertant survivors that 
grew on the low concentration of histidine in the plates. Were there 
millions of survivors, the amount of histidine would have been insuf-
ficient to allow any of them (except real revertants) to form colonies. 
This artifact is easily avoided by checking that there is a faint lawn 
of bacterial growth in the plates; one can also confirm that colonies 
are revertants by streaking them on medium without histidine to be 
sure that they grow in its absence. Such pitfalls exist in all mutagenic-
ity tests. Therefore, anyone performing mutagenicity tests must have 
detailed familiarity with the laboratory application and literature of 
the assay and be observant about the responsiveness of the assay in 
terms of laboratory historical negative and positive control values.
Although information from the Ames assay has become a 
standard in genetic toxicology testing, equivalent information can 
be obtained from other bacterial assays. Like the Ames assay, the 
WP2 tryptophan reversion assay in E. coli (Kirkland et al., 1990; 
Mortelmans and Riccio, 2000) incorporates genetic features that 
enhance assay sensitivity, can accommodate S9 metabolic acti-
vation, and performs well in many laboratories. Mutations are 
detected by selecting for reversion of a trpE allele from Trp− to 
Trp+. Its responsiveness to mutagens most closely resembles TA102 
among the Ames strains (Mortelmans and Riccio, 2000).
Bacterial reversion assays are commonly used for testing pur-
poses, but they also provide information on molecular mechanisms 
of mutagenesis. The broader understanding of mutational mecha-
nisms that comes from refined genetic assays and molecular analy-
sis of mutations can contribute to the interpretation of mutational 
hazards. The primary reversion mechanisms of the Ames strains,

521

summarized in Table 9-3, were initially determined by genetic and 
biochemical means (Maron and Ames, 1983). An ingenious method 
called allele-specific colony hybridization greatly facilitated the 
molecular analysis of revertants in the Ames assay (Koch et al., 
1994), and many spontaneous and induced revertants have been 
cloned or amplified by the polymerase chain reaction (PCR) and 
sequenced (Levine et al., 1994; DeMarini, 2000).
Part III of Table 9-3 is by necessity a simplification with 
respect both to targets and mechanisms of reversion of the Ames 
strains. Some mutations that bring about reversion to histidine 
independence fall outside the primary target, and the full target has 
been found to be as much as 76 base pairs in hisD3052 (DeMarini 
et al., 1998). Other revertants can arise by suppressor mutations in 
other genes. It has been shown that hisG46, hisG428, hisC3076,
